Financial support for foreign nationals in New Zealand --- Section Separator --- Travel to New Zealand Leaving New Zealand Transit through New Zealand Travel within New Zealand New Zealanders overseas Foreign nationals in New Zealand
Home Travel and the border Leaving New Zealand --- Section Separator --- Before travelling overseas we recommend you check the requirements of the country you are travelling to. Check: the official website of the country you wish to enter or transit, or that country’s diplomatic representative in New Zealand. Many countries require travellers to self-isolate for 14 days on arrival, or have a negative COVID-19 test result before they can travel. New Zealanders are advised not to travel overseas at this time. The New Zealand Government has raised its travel advice to “do not travel” — the highest level — regardless of destination. Travel advice at SafeTravel (external link) The New Zealand Government is urging any New Zealanders currently travelling overseas to register with SafeTravel. If you register, you will receive notification of any changes made to travel advice. Register your details with SafeTravel (external link) New Zealanders overseas If you are a foreign national travelling in New Zealand, we recommend you consider returning home as soon as you are able to. Advice for foreign nationals in New Zealand You can travel from New Zealand to some states in Australia without needing to quarantine there if you: have been in New Zealand for 14 days or more and have not been in a designated hotspot, and are travelling to Australia on a quarantine-free flight. If you want to avoid quarantine, you can only travel to: Australian Capital Territory New South Wales Northern Territory, or Victoria. If you want to travel on to other states or territories, you will have to check what you need to do before you travel. Some states or territories may require you to quarantine when you arrive. The Australian Government has more information on: designated hotspots and if there are any in New Zealand quarantine-free flights whether you need a visa to enter Australia travel exemptions if you have been in New Zealand for less than 14 days. Guidance on travelling between New Zealand and Australia from the Australian Government (external link) If you plan to travel back to New Zealand after your trip to Australia, you will need to enter managed isolation or quarantine when you arrive back. You may be charged for your stay. Before booking your flights, you need to register for a voucher to secure your place in a managed isolation facility. Managed isolation and quarantine in New Zealand (external link) Spaces in managed isolation are extremely limited pre-Christmas, but may become available as travel plans change. We recommend you regularly check the Managed Isolation Allocation System website for your preferred date. Managed Isolation Allocation System (external link) Space in managed isolation is extremely limited. This means the criteria for requesting an emergency allocation have been restricted. You can only request an emergency allocation if you meet both the following criteria: You are a New Zealand citizen or resident-class visa holder. You have an imminent threat to your life or serious risk to health, which requires urgent travel to New Zealand. Very few emergency allocation requests will be granted. We encourage you to book in early to the Managed Isolation Allocation System, as this is the best way to guarantee your place. Emergency allocation requests (external link) Some countries require you to have a negative COVID-19 test before you travel. As soon as your travel plans are confirmed, contact Healthline or your GP. Find out when you can go in for a COVID-19 test. The test needs to be taken as close as possible to your date of travel — check the timing that applies to your country. You may need to pay for the test yourself, at the time. Your test will be sent to a local laboratory, which will provide a result to your GP or you directly. A health provider will let you know the result of the test. It can take several days for test results to be available, so make sure you do not leave it too late. Assuming the test is negative, you will be given a hard-copy document with the results to take to the airport. In the unlikely event the test is positive, your health provider will talk with you about what happens next. Take your negative test results with you to airport check-in. You may need to show your results to customs or immigration on arrival at your destination. The New Zealand Government has transit arrangements with a number of countries to make it easier for each country’s citizens to get home. Transit agreements that say who may transit through New Zealand are published by Immigration New Zealand. You should also check if you need to apply for a transit exemption from managed isolation or quarantine. Transit arrangements from Immigration New Zealand (external link) Exemptions to transit through New Zealand, and how to apply (external link) --- Section Separator --- Before travelling overseas we recommend you check the requirements of the country you are travelling to. Check: the official website of the country you wish to enter or transit, or that country’s diplomatic representative in New Zealand. Many countries require travellers to self-isolate for 14 days on arrival, or have a negative COVID-19 test result before they can travel. --- Section Separator --- New Zealanders are advised not to travel overseas at this time. The New Zealand Government has raised its travel advice to “do not travel” — the highest level — regardless of destination. Travel advice at SafeTravel (external link) --- Section Separator --- The New Zealand Government is urging any New Zealanders currently travelling overseas to register with SafeTravel. If you register, you will receive notification of any changes made to travel advice. Register your details with SafeTravel (external link) New Zealanders overseas --- Section Separator --- If you are a foreign national travelling in New Zealand, we recommend you consider returning home as soon as you are able to. Advice for foreign nationals in New Zealand --- Section Separator --- You can travel from New Zealand to some states in Australia without needing to quarantine there if you: have been in New Zealand for 14 days or more and have not been in a designated hotspot, and are travelling to Australia on a quarantine-free flight. If you want to avoid quarantine, you can only travel to: Australian Capital Territory New South Wales Northern Territory, or Victoria. If you want to travel on to other states or territories, you will have to check what you need to do before you travel. Some states or territories may require you to quarantine when you arrive. The Australian Government has more information on: designated hotspots and if there are any in New Zealand quarantine-free flights whether you need a visa to enter Australia travel exemptions if you have been in New Zealand for less than 14 days. Guidance on travelling between New Zealand and Australia from the Australian Government (external link) If you plan to travel back to New Zealand after your trip to Australia, you will need to enter managed isolation or quarantine when you arrive back. You may be charged for your stay. Before booking your flights, you need to register for a voucher to secure your place in a managed isolation facility. Managed isolation and quarantine in New Zealand (external link) --- Section Separator --- Spaces in managed isolation are extremely limited pre-Christmas, but may become available as travel plans change. We recommend you regularly check the Managed Isolation Allocation System website for your preferred date. Managed Isolation Allocation System (external link) --- Section Separator --- Space in managed isolation is extremely limited. This means the criteria for requesting an emergency allocation have been restricted. You can only request an emergency allocation if you meet both the following criteria: You are a New Zealand citizen or resident-class visa holder. You have an imminent threat to your life or serious risk to health, which requires urgent travel to New Zealand. Very few emergency allocation requests will be granted. We encourage you to book in early to the Managed Isolation Allocation System, as this is the best way to guarantee your place. Emergency allocation requests (external link) --- Section Separator --- Some countries require you to have a negative COVID-19 test before you travel. As soon as your travel plans are confirmed, contact Healthline or your GP. Find out when you can go in for a COVID-19 test. The test needs to be taken as close as possible to your date of travel — check the timing that applies to your country. You may need to pay for the test yourself, at the time. Your test will be sent to a local laboratory, which will provide a result to your GP or you directly. A health provider will let you know the result of the test. It can take several days for test results to be available, so make sure you do not leave it too late. Assuming the test is negative, you will be given a hard-copy document with the results to take to the airport. In the unlikely event the test is positive, your health provider will talk with you about what happens next. Take your negative test results with you to airport check-in. You may need to show your results to customs or immigration on arrival at your destination. --- Section Separator --- The New Zealand Government has transit arrangements with a number of countries to make it easier for each country’s citizens to get home. Transit agreements that say who may transit through New Zealand are published by Immigration New Zealand. You should also check if you need to apply for a transit exemption from managed isolation or quarantine. These exceptions apply to people who: already hold a temporary New Zealand visa don’t have a New Zealand visa. What to bring with you to New Zealand (external link) Keep yourself, your whānau and those around you safe while travelling to New Zealand. Avoid going to high-risk events like parties, social gatherings or crowded places. Avoid contact with people who have COVID-19 or who are contacts of people with COVID-19. Stay home as much as possible to limit your contact with other people. Wash and dry your hands often, cough into your elbow and avoid touching your face. Keep your distance from people you do not know. Wear a face covering when you cannot keep your distance. Doing these things will help reduce your risk of being exposed to COVID-19 and bringing it home with you. What happens when you arrive back in New Zealand depends on whether you arrive by air or sea. Arriving in New Zealand and being transferred to an isolation or quarantine facility (external link) Entering managed isolation and what to expect (external link) When you arrive by air, you will be screened for COVID-19 symptoms at the airport. If you have symptoms or are waiting for the results of a test, you will go to a quarantine facility. Otherwise, you will go to an isolation facility. Both types of facilities are in hotels. When you arrive by sea, you may complete your isolation on your vessel. Home Travel and the border Travel to New Zealand Managed isolation and quarantine --- Section Separator --- While our borders are closed, New Zealand citizens and permanent residents have a legal right to come home. Those returning to New Zealand have a responsibility to do their part to stop COVID-19 spreading. By completing 14 days in managed isolation or quarantine, we can ensure people do not have COVID-19 before returning to our communities. People are tested for COVID-19 around day 3 and day 12 of their stay, and they must test negative before they can leave. Over 68,000 people have completed their managed isolation stay safely, and have returned to their loved ones and friends. When you arrive in New Zealand you are screened for symptoms of COVID-19. If you: do not have symptoms, you will isolate at a managed isolation facility have symptoms or test positive for COVID-19, you will isolate in a quarantine facility or separate area. Both types of facilities are located within hotels. You are legally required to have a voucher before flying to New Zealand. Your voucher allocates you a place in a managed isolation facility. Airlines will not be permitted to board you if you do not have a voucher, unless you are exempt from using the Managed Isolation Allocation System. Get your voucher by registering on the Managed Isolation Allocation System. Register for a voucher for managed isolation (external link) Spaces in managed isolation are extremely limited pre-Christmas, but may become available as travel plans change. We recommend you regularly check the Managed Isolation Allocation System website for your preferred date. Managed Isolation Allocation System (external link) Space in managed isolation is extremely limited. This means the criteria for requesting an emergency allocation have been restricted. You can only request an emergency allocation if you meet both the following criteria: You are a New Zealand citizen or resident-class visa holder. You have an imminent threat to your life or serious risk to health, which requires urgent travel to New Zealand. Very few emergency allocation requests will be granted. We encourage you to book in early to the Managed Isolation Allocation System, as this is the best way to guarantee your place. Emergency allocation requests (external link) You do not have to pay for a voucher. However, some people will be charged for their stay in managed isolation or quarantine. The Government recovers some of the costs for managed isolation. This is to share the costs in a way that fairly reflects the benefits to both the New Zealand public of having a robust system, and those who leave and enter the country. Costs for managed isolation and who needs to pay (external link) There are no exemptions from quarantine for people with COVID-19 symptoms. Managed isolation is an important part of keeping COVID-19 out of New Zealand, but we know there are people facing exceptional circumstances. We have put in place a robust system for exemptions from managed isolation. Releases from managed isolation are only issued in very limited circumstances, and where the public health risk is low and can be managed. Any exemption is from being in managed isolation, not from having to isolate. The managed isolation and quarantine website, run by the Ministry of Business, Innovation and Employment (MBIE), has information about exemptions, including: what an exemption is types of exemption available how to apply Exemptions from managed isolation and how to apply (external link) --- Section Separator --- While our borders are closed, New Zealand citizens and permanent residents have a legal right to come home. Advice for when you leave your house including how to lower the risk of transmission. Advice for travelling locally, including public transport, how to travel safely and other ways of travel. Advice for travelling between different regions of New Zealand. --- Section Separator --- Advice for when you leave your house including how to lower the risk of transmission. Advice for travelling locally, including public transport, how to travel safely and other ways of travel. Advice for travelling between different regions of New Zealand.
Home Travel and the border Travel within New Zealand Leaving your house --- Section Separator --- You can leave your house and gather with as many people as you want at Alert Level 1. We encourage you to use face coverings on public transport and when you cannot maintain physical distance from people you do not know, for example in supermarkets. At Alert Level 1, we still need to play it safe. We all need to be ready in case COVID-19 comes back into the community. To be ready at Alert Level 1: maintain good hygiene stay home if you’re sick get tested if you have cold or flu symptoms keep track of where you’ve been and who you’ve seen. Get more advice on living at Alert Level 1, including keeping your distance, wearing face coverings and contact tracing for businesses At Alert Level 2, it's important that you follow public health measures and consider others. Do everything you can to reduce the risk of transmission. COVID-19 is in New Zealand. Play it safe. Keep a 2 metre distance from people you don’t know when you’re in public places and retail stores. In other environments, such as your workplace, try to keep a 1 metre distance from others. Wear a face covering when you cannot maintain physical distance from people you don't know. Wash your hands properly, and dry them. Sneeze and cough into your elbow, and regularly disinfect surfaces. Keep track of where you’ve been and who you’ve seen. Exercising safely There are times when it's important that you not be out and about. If you’re sick, stay home. Don’t go to work or school. Don’t socialise. If you have symptoms of a cold or flu call your doctor or Healthline on 0800 358 5453 and get tested. If you have been told to self-isolate do so immediately. You legally must wear a face covering on public transport. There are some situations when you do not have to wear them. Face coverings on public transport information If you’re over 70 or are at higher risk of severe illness from COVID-19, take extra care when you leave your house. Guidance for at-risk people At Alert Level 3, we recommend people stay at home as much as possible except for exercise, going to work or school, or visiting local services like the doctor, pharmacy or supermarket. If you’re over 70 or are at higher risk of severe illness should you contract COVID-19, you need to be especially careful. Guidance for at-risk people at Alert Level 3 Keep a 2 metre distance from other people at all times. Don’t touch surfaces others may have touched — avoid park benches or playgrounds Don’t travel far from home, especially not to baches or second homes Only shop for essential supplies Wash your hands regularly Stay local if you go out for exercise. More information about exercising safely You legally must wear a face covering on public transport. There are some situations when you do not have to wear them. Face coverings on public transport information Staying close to home is one of the most important things we can do to reduce the risk of spreading the disease. Your local area means the area near your home that you regularly visit for essential services. What is considered “local” will differ depending on where you live. City dwellers may have a supermarket or dairy close by. If you live rurally, you may need to take a drive to reach these. You can exercise at parks or beaches within your region, but the closer to home the better. Be safe while out exercising. Keep 2 metres away from anybody not in your bubble. Make minimal trips. Most of all during this time, we encourage you to continue to connect with one another. Stay in touch with your older relatives and vulnerable people, talk to your friends, whānau and neighbours over the phone and see if they need your support. Acts of kindness and maintaining these connections is essential while we work to stop the spread of COVID-19. --- Section Separator --- You can leave your house and gather with as many people as you want at Alert Level 1. We encourage you to use face coverings on public transport and when you cannot maintain physical distance from people you do not know, for example in supermarkets. --- Section Separator --- At Alert Level 1, we still need to play it safe. We all need to be ready in case COVID-19 comes back into the community. To be ready at Alert Level 1: maintain good hygiene stay home if you’re sick get tested if you have cold or flu symptoms keep track of where you’ve been and who you’ve seen. Get more advice on living at Alert Level 1, including keeping your distance, wearing face coverings and contact tracing for businesses --- Section Separator --- At Alert Level 2, it's important that you follow public health measures and consider others. Do everything you can to reduce the risk of transmission. COVID-19 is in New Zealand. Play it safe. Keep a 2 metre distance from people you don’t know when you’re in public places and retail stores. In other environments, such as your workplace, try to keep a 1 metre distance from others. Wear a face covering when you cannot maintain physical distance from people you don't know. Wash your hands properly, and dry them. Sneeze and cough into your elbow, and regularly disinfect surfaces. Keep track of where you’ve been and who you’ve seen. Exercising safely There are times when it's important that you not be out and about. If you’re sick, stay home. Don’t go to work or school. Don’t socialise. If you have symptoms of a cold or flu call your doctor or Healthline on 0800 358 5453 and get tested. If you have been told to self-isolate do so immediately. You legally must wear a face covering on public transport. There are some situations when you do not have to wear them. Face coverings on public transport information If you’re over 70 or are at higher risk of severe illness from COVID-19, take extra care when you leave your house. Guidance for at-risk people --- Section Separator --- At Alert Level 3, we recommend people stay at home as much as possible except for exercise, going to work or school, or visiting local services like the doctor, pharmacy or supermarket. If you’re over 70 or are at higher risk of severe illness should you contract COVID-19, you need to be especially careful. Guidance for at-risk people at Alert Level 3 Keep a 2 metre distance from other people at all times. Don’t touch surfaces others may have touched — avoid park benches or playgrounds Don’t travel far from home, especially not to baches or second homes Only shop for essential supplies Wash your hands regularly Stay local if you go out for exercise. More information about exercising safely You legally must wear a face covering on public transport. There are some situations when you do not have to wear them. Face coverings on public transport information Staying close to home is one of the most important things we can do to reduce the risk of spreading the disease. Your local area means the area near your home that you regularly visit for essential services. What is considered “local” will differ depending on where you live. City dwellers may have a supermarket or dairy close by. If you live rurally, you may need to take a drive to reach these. You can exercise at parks or beaches within your region, but the closer to home the better. Be safe while out exercising. Keep 2 metres away from anybody not in your bubble. Make minimal trips. Most of all during this time, we encourage you to continue to connect with one another. Stay in touch with your older relatives and vulnerable people, talk to your friends, whānau and neighbours over the phone and see if they need your support. Acts of kindness and maintaining these connections is essential while we work to stop the spread of COVID-19. --- Section Separator --- Leaving your house Local travel Regional travel
Home Travel and the border Travel within New Zealand Local travel --- Section Separator --- Face coverings legally must be worn: on all public transport to, from and through Auckland — including on long-distance bus and train journeys, as well as Auckland ferries on domestic flights throughout New Zealand by Auckland taxi and ride-share drivers — while it’s not compulsory for passengers to wear them, we strongly encourage you to. Wear a face covering At Alert Level 1, you can travel but try to do it in a safe way. Public transport operators legally must display QR code posters to help you keep track of where you’ve been. We recommend you use the NZ COVID Tracer app to do this, or another way that works for you. Face coverings legally must be worn: on all public transport to, from and through Auckland — including on long-distance bus and train journeys, as well as Auckland ferries on domestic flights throughout New Zealand by Auckland taxi and ride-share drivers — while it’s not compulsory for passengers to wear them, we strongly encourage you to. There are exemptions for some people. Wear a face covering You legally must wear a face covering when you’re travelling anywhere on a domestic flight in New Zealand — this does not include private flights. There is still a risk of COVID-19 returning to the community at Alert Level 1. We encourage you to continue using a face covering on public transport. Wear a face covering At Alert Level 1, we still need to play it safe. We all need to be ready in case COVID-19 comes back into the community. To be ready at Alert Level 1: maintain good hygiene stay home if you’re sick get tested if you have cold or flu symptoms keep track of where you’ve been and who you’ve seen. Get more advice on living at Alert Level 1, including keeping your distance, wearing face coverings and contact tracing for businesses At Alert Level 2, you can travel, but make sure you do it in a safe way: Follow hygiene practices. Keep records of what travel services you use. Keep track of who you have been in contact with. Keep your distance from groups of people you don’t know, where possible. You legally must wear a face covering on public transport. At other times, consider wearing a face covering when you cannot maintain physical distance from people you don't know. Minimise the number of places you stop on the way to your destination. Do not travel to events which do not meet the requirements for gatherings at Alert Level 2. Tips for minimising risk on public transport: Avoid sitting or standing next to someone you don’t know, where possible. Don't travel if you are displaying symptoms of COVID-19, awaiting a test or if you need to self-isolate. You legally must wear a face covering on public transport. There are some situations when you do not have to wear them. Face coverings on public transport information You can use a private vehicle for transport. Please take care around anyone cycling or walking. Slow down and save lives. Walking and other active transport like cycling or scootering are fine. Keep a 2 metre distance from other pedestrians. Take extra care if you need to step out into the street as there will be more traffic at Alert Level 2. If you’re healthy and don't have COVID-19 related symptoms, you can use taxis and ride-sharing services to travel anywhere in New Zealand. Guidelines when travelling by taxi or ride-share: Stay home if you're sick. Wash your hands before and after travelling. Cough and sneeze into your elbow. Keep a record of which service you travelled with and when. Apart from the driver, only travel with people you know, for example friends and family. Minimise the number of stops on the way to your destination. Consider wearing a face covering when you cannot maintain physical distance from people you don't know. Drivers of taxis and ride-sharing servic es need to wear a face covering. At Alert Level 3, travel is still restricted and is only allowed for permitted movement in your local area — for example going to work or school, shopping, or getting exercise. You are strongly encouraged to wear a face covering when you're outside your home and in a place where it’s hard to stay 2 metres away from other people, like in shops. Don’t travel outside of your region unless your local area crosses a regional boundary. Your local area means the area near your home that you regularly visit for essential services. What is considered local will differ depending on where you live. City dwellers may have a supermarket or dairy close by. If you live rurally, you may need to take a drive to reach these. Remember, any unnecessary travel may spread COVID-19. You can use public transport to travel within your local area, but only for the following reasons: accessing local services and businesses going to work or school, but only if you can't stay at home low-risk exercise in your local area visiting people in your extended bubble travelling to permitted gatherings. You legally must wear a face covering on public transport. There are some situations when you do not have to wear them. Face coverings on public transport information We recommend you only travel in your car with people from your bubble. If you have to drive for essential reasons, please take care around anyone cycling or walking. Slow down and save lives. Walking and other active transport like cycling or scootering are fine, provided you keep a 2 metre distance from anybody outside of your bubble. If you don't have COVID-19 related symptoms, you can use taxis and ride-sharing services for only for the following reasons: accessing local services and businesses going to work or school for those who have to low-risk exercise in your local area visiting people in your extended bubble travelling to permitted gatherings. You are strongly encouraged to wear a face covering when you're in a taxi or ride-share. To help limit the spread of any virus, we recommend those who are unwell stay at home. If you're unwell and must travel, private transport is required. If that's not possible, call Healthline for guidance first on 0800 358 5453 . All driver licences, warrants of fitness (WoFs), certificates of fitness (CoFs) and some vehicle certifications that expired on or after 1 January 2020 were extended until 10 October. This recognised that during Alert Level 4 and Alert Level 3, through no fault of their own, many New Zealanders were unable to keep these documents up to date. This extension allowed vehicle users extra time to allow them to get up to date. As well as general hygiene guidance, there are some specific requirements while travelling in a taxi or ride-share. Please also follow any requests from the driver. Where possible, use hand sanitiser before and after you enter or exit a vehicle. When you get home, wash your hands for at least 20 seconds with soap and water and dry them thoroughly. Physical distancing between the driver and passengers is important, even in smaller vehicles. If you're a passenger, sit in the rear passenger seats only — avoid sitting in the front passenger seat next to the driver. Sit as far as possible from the driver. If you’re the sole passenger, sit in the rear left-hand side passenger seat, diagonally opposite the driver. The maximum number of passengers is the number of passenger seats in the back of the vehicle. Most cars normally have 3 rear passenger seats. You’re encouraged to wear a face covering when you’re in a taxi or ride-share. Drivers of taxis and ride-sharing services in Auckland legally must wear a face covering. If you are a passenger in a taxi of ride-share, we strongly encourage you to wear a face covering. Please follow any requests for providing contact tracing information if not already provided, for example, electronically through an app or booking system. Home Travel and the border Travel within New Zealand Regional travel --- Section Separator --- Face coverings legally must be worn: on all public transport to, from and through Auckland — including on long-distance bus and train journeys, as well as Auckland ferries on domestic flights throughout New Zealand by Auckland taxi and ride-share drivers — while it’s not compulsory for passengers to wear them, we strongly encourage you to. Wear a face covering If you’re living at Alert Level 1, you can travel anywhere in New Zealand that is at Alert Level 1 or 2. If you travel to a region that is at Alert Level 2, make sure you follow the restrictions and advice for that region. Use the NZ COVID Tracer app to keep track of where you've been while travelling or on holiday, as you may not recall all the places you've visited. Face coverings legally must be worn: on all public transport to, from and through Auckland — including on long-distance bus and train journeys, as well as Auckland ferries on domestic flights throughout New Zealand by Auckland taxi and ride-share drivers — while it’s not compulsory for passengers to wear them, we strongly encourage you to. There are exemptions for some people. Even when it is not a legal requirement, we encourage you to wear face coverings on public transport. Wear a face covering If you're sick, stay home and do not travel. If you become unwell while on holiday or travelling away from home, call Healthline on 0800 358 5453 or your GP or Nurse Practitioner to find out if you should get a test. If you're advised to get a test, do so from wherever you are in the country. Do not wait until you get home to get a test. Stay home if you're sick At Alert Level 1, we still need to play it safe. We all need to be ready in case COVID-19 comes back into the community. To be ready at Alert Level 1: maintain good hygiene stay home if you’re sick get tested if you have cold or flu symptoms keep track of where you’ve been and who you’ve seen. Get more advice on living at Alert Level 1, including keeping your distance, wearing face coverings and contact tracing for businesses If you’re living at Alert Level 2, you can travel anywhere in New Zealand that is at Alert Level 1 or 2, but make sure you do it in a safe way. You legally must wear a face covering when travelling to, from or through an Alert Level 2 region on public transport, including flights. If you travel to a region that is at Alert Level 1, we encourage you to act as if you are carrying your Alert Level with you. Consider avoiding large gatherings, unless you’re going to a funeral or tangihanga. If you’re sick, please don’t travel at all — stay home and get tested. You legally must wear a face covering on public transport and aircraft. There are exceptions. Face coverings Keep records of what travel services you use. Keep track of who you have been in contact with. Keep your distance from groups of people you don’t know, where possible. Do not travel: if you have symptoms of COVID-19, are awaiting a test or if you need to self-isolate to events which do not meet the requirements for gatherings at Alert Level 2. You can take a domestic flight for recreation and tourism. Follow guidance to stay safe. Track your journey. Follow all the guidance from transport operators and crew. Sit only in your allocated seat onboard the aircraft. Maintain physical distancing where you can. You can fly a small aircraft or glider for recreation if you can do it safely. Keep a record of your journey. You can use the Cook Strait ferries for recreation and tourism. Follow guidance to stay safe, track your journey and follow these tips: Follow the instructions and guidance from crew and transport operators. Maintain physical distancing where you can. Comply with any temporary barriers and seat signage telling you where you’re allowed to sit. Maintain good hygiene all the time — wash your hands regularly, sneeze or cough into your elbow. --- Section Separator --- Face coverings legally must be worn: on all public transport to, from and through Auckland — including on long-distance bus and train journeys, as well as Auckland ferries on domestic flights throughout New Zealand by Auckland taxi and ride-share drivers — while it’s not compulsory for passengers to wear them, we strongly encourage you to. Home Updates and resources Accessible information Easy Read --- Section Separator --- Easy Read information uses words and pictures to present information in a way that’s easier to understand. Easy Read information has been developed to support people with learning disabilities better understand written information. Easy Read information might be helpful for people who: have a learning disability have low literacy levels use English as a second language are elderly are Deaf. Home Updates and resources Contact a helpline --- Section Separator --- Call 111 immediately in an emergency. If you have COVID-19 symptoms, call the dedicated COVID-19 Healthline: for free on 0800 358 5453 on +64 9 358 5453 if you have an international SIM. For any other health concerns, call the general Healthline number on 0800 611 116 . All calls to Healthline are free and someone is available 24 hours a day, 7 days a week. For support with anxiety, distress or mental wellbeing, call or text 1737 to talk with a trained counsellor for free, 24 hours a day, 7 days a week. There are helplines available that offer support, information and help. All services are available 24 hours a day, 7 days a week. Mental wellbeing helplines Help and support is available if you are experiencing family violence or sexual violence. Family and sexual violence prevention helplines A free helpline service is available for all New Zealand businesses. It can offer you: specific advice and access to Government business support advice on what the current Alert Level means for your business HR and general business advice and access to online resources and webinars connections to business advisory services. To find out more call 0800 500 362 for North Island, or 0800 50 50 96 for South Island. If you have questions or feedback about the NZ COVID Tracer app: call 0800 800 606 email help@covidtracer.min.health.nz If you have questions about managed isolation and quarantine, including charges and exemptions: call 0800 ISOMIQ ( 0800 476 647 ) from within New Zealand call +64 4 888 1670 from outside New Zealand or if you’re in New Zealand with an international SIM email enquiries@miq.govt.nz Many government agencies have language support available, if English is not your first language and you need an interpreter. When you call a government agency for information, ask for an interpreter in your language and wait to be connected. Information and advice about COVID-19 is available in many languages If you’re deaf, hearing impaired, Deafblind or speech impaired you can use the New Zealand Relay Service to call. New Zealand Relay Services (external link) --- Section Separator --- Call 111 immediately in an emergency. --- Section Separator --- If you have COVID-19 symptoms, call the dedicated COVID-19 Healthline: for free on 0800 358 5453 on +64 9 358 5453 if you have an international SIM. Home Updates and resources Data and statistics --- Section Separator --- The Ministry of Health collects data about cases of COVID-19, clusters, locations of interest and testing in New Zealand. You can get data on: the number of confirmed and probable cases of COVID-19 the location and source of active cases cases by district health board (DHB). Current COVID-19 cases (external link) You can get data on confirmed and probable cases by: ethnicity age and sex hospitalisation and intensive care COVID-19 case demographics (external link) Data on how the virus has spread in New Zealand, for example imported cases, locally acquired but linked to another case, or locally acquired with no known source. You can also get data on clusters of cases. Source of COVID-19 cases (external link) The Ministry of Health publishes data on: total tests completed tests by location, for example in managed isolation or by district health board tests by ethnicity testing rates, including testing by location, ethnicity, and age and sex. COVID-19 testing data (external link) Locations of interest are places where someone confirmed with COVID-19 visited. If you visited a location of interest at a certain time, you may be classed as a ‘casual contact’. The Ministry of Health lists locations of interest and has advice on what you should do if you are a casual contact. Locations of interest in New Zealand (external link) Data and statistics about managed isolation and quarantine is published by the Ministry of Business, Innovation and Employment (MBIE). The data includes: daily data updates projected occupancy over the next 14 days number of people who have returned to New Zealand daily incident reports testing data and current COVID-19 cases in managed isolation and quarantine. Managed isolation and quarantine data (external link) The Ministry of Health publishes a series of modelling reports, which help us understand the future health impact of COVID-19 in New Zealand. COVID-19 modelling reports on the Ministry of Health website (external link) --- Section Separator --- The Ministry of Health collects data about cases of COVID-19, clusters, locations of interest and testing in New Zealand. Transcript of the media conference --- Section Separator --- Update on today's cases from the Ministry of Health (external link)
Home Updates and resources Latest updates Transcript of 21 October media conference --- Section Separator --- Kia ora koutou katoa , Welcome to today’s briefing.  I’ve got Dr Caroline McElnay, the Director of Public Health, with me here today to answer all the difficult questions and to support me here.  Unfortunately as you can see no sign language interpreter here but I can say there will be a limited transcript of the live webcast on the Ministry’s YouTube channel and a full transcript will be available as soon as possible after we have finished. So today there are twenty-five new cases of COVID-19 to report, twenty-three of these are imported cases at the border, and two are cases linked to the port worker that we reported on Sunday and they are workplace contacts. I will start with those workplace contacts.  The first case that I am reporting today was previously considered a casual contact of the port worker when we announced that case on Sunday.  This person who is in the small firm that the initial case works for, had a very short exposure to the case on Friday morning when they became symptomatic, that is the initial case, really we are talking about a few minutes in the same room.  This new case that we are reporting today became symptomatic yesterday and rightly was tested and has returned a positive result.  They have one household contact who is also now being tested and will be in self-isolation for the fourteen-day period. The second case that is a workplace contact actually came into our database after 9am, however we are making an exception and we are reporting the case today because there is obviously a lot of interest in this particular case and it is important that people know as soon as we have the information. So this person is a previously reported workplace contact of the initial port work reported on Sunday and the person has been at the Jet Park since Sunday.  They had been tested on Friday as part of the routine workplace testing at the port and returned a negative test and that was on top of a number of previously negative tests as part of the routine surveillance testing at the port.  The person developed symptoms yesterday and has now returned a positive result. His household contacts were contacts of contacts but we had as an extra precaution isolated and tested them anyway.  They had returned negative test results and are now considered close contacts.  They will complete the full fourteen days isolation and be retested as part of that routinely or if they develop symptoms. And obviously further interviews are underway with both of our two new cases.  They are both well contained and had already been spoken with but just to see if there is any further contact tracing that needs to happen.  And we will update that if there is further information. Infection via the Sofrana Surville ship, the original case worked on, continues to be the most likely hypothesis for where this infection originated but of course we are keeping an open mind on that.  The ship is currently in Brisbane and testing has been done.  I don’t have any testing results to give today.  If they come in we will report them, if we get some results today we will report them out of cycle.  There are nineteen crew members and we will report them as soon as they become available. I’m moving now to the situation in the Christchurch Sudima managed isolation facility which we reported on yesterday afternoon.  When we reported yesterday afternoon we had identified eleven cases.  There are now a total of eighteen cases in that facility amongst the Russian and Ukrainian fishing boat crews who all came in on a chartered flight that originated in Moscow, flew to Singapore, they remained on board in Singapore, then flew through to Christchurch and have been in that managed isolation which is dedicated just to that group of two hundred and thirty-five.  They were tested pre-departure and in fact two people didn’t get on the flight out of Moscow because they returned a positive test. Our current information is that that testing was not rapid testing, it was the nasopharyngeal swabs with PCR testing.  We have now got a number of the actual results from that testing and we are going through just checking and matching with those who we now have as positive results.  The initial ??? referencing of the threshold cycle values is that some of those cases that we have identified in that eighteen are new acute cases and some are probably historical infections just looking at the CT values but we are also doing serology testing to help us build the picture there. Those positive cases have now all been moved into a dedicated quarantine wing in the same hotel, in the Sudima.  There were rooms set aside if they were required.  Anyone who was in a room with a case is obviously also being followed up as a close contact.  They are now in single rooms.  Most of the people in that group are actually in twin rooms in the hotel at the moment and we are looking at the risks of potentially moving them into single rooms and including whether we need to use additional facilities and we will be talking with our infection prevention and control colleagues down there in managed isolation facilities. We are adding in a additional day six testing for all those people and that will happen tomorrow because we want to be sure that there hasn’t been cross-infection either on the flight or inside the facility so we will add that in, and we are fully expecting that this group may need to stay for additional days beyond the fourteen days just because of the number of infections and the nature of the double two people in one room arrangements they have been in there. All staff at the facility have been tested over the last three days and we are going to add in some additional testing for staff members, and their families are also being offered testing should they wish to take that up.  People at the airport where the travellers arrived into which was obviously Christchurch airport are being asked to be tested if they haven’t already been tested in the last couple of days and the bus company that transported them to the managed isolation facility from the airport is testing its drivers. We have some other imported cases to make up our total of what is possibly a record twenty-five cases.  We haven’t had that number of cases in a while. So there are three unrelated imported cases who arrived from London via Singapore cases on the 16th October and tested positive at routine day 3 testing at managed isolation in Christchurch. What we are now doing, I might have mentioned this yesterday, is we now have a little investigation on every client in managed isolation, looking particularly at the CT values just to see if these are likely to be acute or historic cases, particularly as we have had a number of recent cases that have only been picked up at day twelve.  And obviously the thing we are alert for is a positive day twelve test raises the possibility of some cross-infection inside the managed isolation facility so I am wanting to rule that out by checking if these cases have a high CT value and are asymptomatic it is very strongly suggestive of a historical case which again reduces the possibility there is cross-infection inside the facility. So in addition to those two other cases in managed isolation, we have a further two imported cases in MIQ facilities in Auckland. The first of these a person that arrived on 16th October from Jordan via Dubai and Kuala Lumpur and tested positive at routine day 3 testing. The second person and the final case that I am talking about today, arrived on the 17th October from Malaysia and tested positive also at routine day 3 testing. Both those cases will now go into the Auckland quarantine facility if they are not there already. So in summary, and it is rather stating the obvious, but we have a large number of cases today, two are contacts, one close and casual contacts of the community case that we announced on Sunday.  All appropriate precautions have been put in place there.  The others are all in managed isolation facilities, a number of them in a single facility, and associated with that charter flight for the fishing crews that were brought in and have been captured through our procedures at the border and are well contained.  And that is exactly why we have the protocols and procedures we have in place so we find these cases and contain them at the border. So just a summary then of the overall numbers, two previously reported cases have now considered to be recovered, and this brings our total number of active cases now to fifty-six.  The total number of confirmed cases is 1,556.  Yesterday the labs processed 6,308 tests and the grand total now is date to 1,040,911. I think that all of these cases, including the case we identified and announced over the weekend who became symptomatic on Friday as well as one of our contacts who became symptomatic yesterday reinforces our critical message, if you have symptoms do not go about your usual business, get a test, and that is a really key part of how we will continue to have an effective COVID-19 response.  Getting a test quickly allows us to rapidly identify any contacts, isolate and test them, and that is instrumental in us being able to contain any potential onward community spread.  It is also important that people continue to keep a record of where they have been.  Please use the NZ COVID app.  I know that for those of you who do, it is always a surprise when you go back and look and think gosh, I wouldn’t have remembered that I did that thirteen days ago. And my final comment is just again putting our twenty-five cases today in perspective, yesterday Spain announced around 48,000 new cases and that would be equivalent of New Zealand announcing nearly 4,000 cases in one day.  Spain is among a number of European countries that are experiencing well over a thousand new infections every day and they are implementing quite significant restrictions on movement and or going back into quite significant lockdowns, Ireland being a good example of the latter. So we have a significant pandemic, it is not going away anywhere soon.  We have done well so far, but all that matters now is what we do now and what we do next, and we all need to play our part in that.  We cannot afford to be complacent.  We are not being complacent at the border, and in managed isolation, and all New Zealanders need to make sure we are not being complacent in our community here in New Zealand.  We want to remain in the current state we are which is a high level of confidence we have eliminated community transmission of COVID-19.  I am now open to questions. Media: You said New Zealand went over and above the regulations regarding testing crew before departure, would you now like to see that added to regulations for essential workers coming out to New Zealand? Dr Bloomfield: Well I think what we have got here is a really good opportunity to look at the value that pre-departure testing has or not.  And one of the things that is quite pertinent here and we are going through all the results, but it seems that all these workers did have pre-departure testing.  Now often that has to happen a few days before departure, especially if it is a PCR test, to get a result before they embark, and now we have clearly a number of positive results at day three that were negative at pre-departure testing, so what it suggests is that pre-departure testing may add some value but it won’t change anything that we do at this end and so I think the regime that we have with our fourteen days in managed isolation plus our days three and day twelve testing continues to be the mainstay of how we will keep the virus out of New Zealand. Media: Are there tight restrictions for the next lot of fishermen coming through? Dr Bloomfield: Are there tight restrictions?  We will look at that.  At this point in time, again the system is working here, and whilst there may still be the departure test it won’t change anything we do at this end, we will still be following absolutely rigid infection control or protocols and testing. Media: Those taking the test, can you explain how reliable those tests are? Dr Bloomfield: Well this is one of the challenges around pre-departure testing,  We don’t have any ability to test the validity of either the process, or the swabbing, or the laboratory or any rapid testing process so it does seem that the testing, and Caroline might want to comment on that, the nature of the testing that’s done. Dr McElnay: We are actually following that up with them.  From the information we have got it does appear to be a PCR test, a nasopharyngeal swab, and the real value of this pre-departure testing is when they test positive because obviously that’s what happened in this situation, and individuals then were not allowed to board the plane and come to New Zealand but for the negatives we just do not have access to that information to say really what is the accuracy of that test in that country Media: And are officials there being quite forthcoming with the information for you? Dr McElnay: I am not aware that there has been any issue. Media: Does the PCR testing … Dr Bloomfield: What it indicates is if they returned a negative test there if it was a good test in Moscow they got infected sometime after they were swabbed.  Now that could have been before they boarded the plane.  At least one must have been aboard that plane who was infectious and there may have been transmission on board the plane.  There may have been transmission once they arrived here.  But the important thing is we did everything the same anyway and the fact that we found these infections is evidence that the system worked. Media: ??? isolation Dr Bloomfield: My understanding is they self-isolated but again I think that, you know, our view would be of what self-isolation or quarantine arrangements might have been in place before people hopped on to the plane and whatever the testing results of the crew they are treated exactly the same when they come here because we have got real confidence in our systems here, both in the integrity of the managed isolation and the value of the day three and day twelve and any additional testing there needs to be, as well as the screening Media: Given that and the likelihood of it happening before coming here, the likelihood that someone was infectious on the plane, does that tell indicate that perhaps everybody was not self-isolating Dr Bloomfield: I couldn’t pass judgement on that but our assumption is and we know that even for the people with the best supportive environment it is quite hard to self-isolate really rigorously to the same degree of integrity that we know that we have in managed isolation.  And with that constant use of PPE, and the real discipline around infection prevention and control, the cleaning and so on, that is one of the key reasons why in early April we moved from self-isolation at home here in New Zealand to self-isolation in managed isolation. Media: Are you still comfortable that this kind of fishermen should come to New Zealand? Dr Bloomfield: Ah well at this point and we are liaising with both our fishery colleagues and obviously those in charge in managed isolation, we will learn again from this particular scenario.  The key thing will be the timing of that flight because we will need to make sure the same facility will be used so we need to make sure that we are confident that before anyone leaves that facility that with anyone that is there at the moment that they are free of the virus. Media: This cohort, any in medical care or needing hospitalisation?  Any communication with Christchurch Hospital over it? Dr McElnay: I haven’t had any of that information yet.  So far my understanding is that they are all able to be managed in the quarantine facility in Christchurch. Dr McElnay: They have all had their first round of testing which is how we have picked up the positive and then those who tested negative will have another test, a second round of testing tomorrow, day six, and they will proceed from there.  There may be some further positives tomorrow. Media: Yesterday Sealord said the Ministry of Health did not contact the infections, they learnt about them from the media.  Why is this? Dr Bloomfield: I would have to look into that, in fact one of the reasons I became aware of this is because my understanding was Sealord had already sent some communication out so regardless of how people found out the system worked, we got positive test results, and I have a detailed timeline of when folk in Christchurch notified our team which was about ten minutes after they had found out the results and you will have seen that out of cycle we actually made an announcement yesterday afternoon because of the importance of getting this information out there. Media: When did the fishermen come into isolation in the facility? Dr Bloomfield: It was Friday afternoon that their flight arrived and so effectively that is their day zero but because of these infections we will be looking at whether actually the clock may need to be re-set because we have had this quite large number of infections. Media: Do you generally trust the Russian authorities to give you straight answers about their COVID testing results? Media: Do you think in the future maybe you will look at other countries to bring their fishing crews from, is that a consideration? Dr Bloomfield: Not from my perspective because that is up to the companies and remembering that this arrangement had been very carefully planned and put in place with our colleagues from MPI. The approval had been provided by Ministers for this to happen, and there is another crew.  Remembering that they are placing crews on these New Zealand fishing boats and some of them have been on those boats for anything up to six – twelve months because of the COVID pandemic and that is why we have been very rigorous in the planning to make sure that we were reducing any risks to the crews and of course to New Zealanders. Media: Russia has had one of the largest outbreaks in the world and Russia has been accused of fudging the numbers right through the pandemic, so is it appropriate to be seeking crews from Russia? Dr Bloomfield: We have got people coming into New Zealand, mostly New Zealanders, from all around the globe including Europe, and from the USA which have large numbers. USA has the largest number of cases of any country in the world.  We treat everybody the same because we know that our procedures are in place to reduce the risk as low as possible of any infection getting into the country. Media: Do you know from the CT values which ones are most likely to be a historical infection versus new or in category where it is hard to tell? Dr McElnay: We don’t have that information but our public health colleagues in Christchurch will have that information and will be working through that. Dr Bloomfield: Maybe you could talk generally about the values and where is the most likely acute and most likely historical Dr McElnay: Certainly our observation is that from the CT values a high CT value can certainly suggest it is more likely to be an historic case and when we have had those, particularly ones that have appeared in the community where for some reason people have been tested, they have got a high CT value and intensive investigations around that person and ceretology we’ve been able to show and we’ve been reporting these for the last few weeks, we’ve been able to demonstrate that they are a historical case. In a situation like this, the need to investigate would really be to help determine who are contacts of cases of that historic case, you don’t have an issue to do with infectiousness of that individual so that is why this particular group would be a mixture of looking at who is historic versus those that are acute.  And for the acute ones that is particularly because they are actively infectious.  I want to reiterate what Ashley said earlier, our quarantine and isolation facilities are set up to manage infection prevention and control so we would assume that anyone could potentially be infectious at any time. Media: If it is a casual contact out of the cluster, is that ??? Media: Scan numbers for the tracer app have been going down for a while now, what is the Ministry doing to get people back to using that? Dr Bloomfield: Well I think a move to level two would have been something that we might have advised when we first found the case if we were not clear where it had come from or if we had found some other cases coming out that we weren’t aware of but in this case we have a really strong line of inquiry for where this person might have become infected and we were able to identify very quickly their close contacts and isolate those people.  So, I think that is the system working as it should in the community. Dr McElnay: Just to add to that, every case has its own case investigation and it may be that the case that we reported today that had a common exposure to the one reported over the weekend and had gone to the same ship, it may be that there is something else in that individual’s history that gives us a stronger line of inquiry, those are the sorts of questions that Regional Public Health will be going over today just in case it looks like the Sofrana is not the source of infection. Media: Does it shine any more light for you on the Americold case that this is the possible source of infection for the three ships Dr Bloomfield: No it doesn’t shine any more light on that.  What it does confirm is that the virus can come in through anywhere in the border at any point in time and that is why we have continually strengthened our arrangements including the very now well-established surveillance testing of workers at sea ports and at airports. --- Section Separator --- Kia ora koutou katoa , Welcome to today’s briefing.  I’ve got Dr Caroline McElnay, the Director of Public Health, with me here today to answer all the difficult questions and to support me here.  Unfortunately as you can see no sign language interpreter here but I can say there will be a limited transcript of the live webcast on the Ministry’s YouTube channel and a full transcript will be available as soon as possible after we have finished. His household contacts were contacts of contacts but we had as an extra precaution isolated and tested them anyway.  They had returned negative test results and are now considered close contacts.  They will complete the full fourteen days isolation and be retested as part of that routinely or if they develop symptoms.
