Infection via the Sofrana Surville ship, the original case worked on, continues to be the most likely hypothesis for where this infection originated but of course we are keeping an open mind on that.  The ship is currently in Brisbane and testing has been done.  I don’t have any testing results to give today.  If they come in we will report them, if we get some results today we will report them out of cycle.  There are nineteen crew members and we will report them as soon as they become available. I’m moving now to the situation in the Christchurch Sudima managed isolation facility which we reported on yesterday afternoon.  When we reported yesterday afternoon we had identified eleven cases.  There are now a total of eighteen cases in that facility amongst the Russian and Ukrainian fishing boat crews who all came in on a chartered flight that originated in Moscow, flew to Singapore, they remained on board in Singapore, then flew through to Christchurch and have been in that managed isolation which is dedicated just to that group of two hundred and thirty-five.  They were tested pre-departure and in fact two people didn’t get on the flight out of Moscow because they returned a positive test. Our current information is that that testing was not rapid testing, it was the nasopharyngeal swabs with PCR testing.  We have now got a number of the actual results from that testing and we are going through just checking and matching with those who we now have as positive results.  The initial ??? referencing of the threshold cycle values is that some of those cases that we have identified in that eighteen are new acute cases and some are probably historical infections just looking at the CT values but we are also doing serology testing to help us build the picture there. Those positive cases have now all been moved into a dedicated quarantine wing in the same hotel, in the Sudima.  There were rooms set aside if they were required.  Anyone who was in a room with a case is obviously also being followed up as a close contact.  They are now in single rooms.  Most of the people in that group are actually in twin rooms in the hotel at the moment and we are looking at the risks of potentially moving them into single rooms and including whether we need to use additional facilities and we will be talking with our infection prevention and control colleagues down there in managed isolation facilities. We are adding in a additional day six testing for all those people and that will happen tomorrow because we want to be sure that there hasn’t been cross-infection either on the flight or inside the facility so we will add that in, and we are fully expecting that this group may need to stay for additional days beyond the fourteen days just because of the number of infections and the nature of the double two people in one room arrangements they have been in there. All staff at the facility have been tested over the last three days and we are going to add in some additional testing for staff members, and their families are also being offered testing should they wish to take that up.  People at the airport where the travellers arrived into which was obviously Christchurch airport are being asked to be tested if they haven’t already been tested in the last couple of days and the bus company that transported them to the managed isolation facility from the airport is testing its drivers. We have some other imported cases to make up our total of what is possibly a record twenty-five cases.  We haven’t had that number of cases in a while. So there are three unrelated imported cases who arrived from London via Singapore cases on the 16th October and tested positive at routine day 3 testing at managed isolation in Christchurch. What we are now doing, I might have mentioned this yesterday, is we now have a little investigation on every client in managed isolation, looking particularly at the CT values just to see if these are likely to be acute or historic cases, particularly as we have had a number of recent cases that have only been picked up at day twelve.  And obviously the thing we are alert for is a positive day twelve test raises the possibility of some cross-infection inside the managed isolation facility so I am wanting to rule that out by checking if these cases have a high CT value and are asymptomatic it is very strongly suggestive of a historical case which again reduces the possibility there is cross-infection inside the facility. So in addition to those two other cases in managed isolation, we have a further two imported cases in MIQ facilities in Auckland. The first of these a person that arrived on 16th October from Jordan via Dubai and Kuala Lumpur and tested positive at routine day 3 testing. The second person and the final case that I am talking about today, arrived on the 17th October from Malaysia and tested positive also at routine day 3 testing. Both those cases will now go into the Auckland quarantine facility if they are not there already. So in summary, and it is rather stating the obvious, but we have a large number of cases today, two are contacts, one close and casual contacts of the community case that we announced on Sunday.  All appropriate precautions have been put in place there.  The others are all in managed isolation facilities, a number of them in a single facility, and associated with that charter flight for the fishing crews that were brought in and have been captured through our procedures at the border and are well contained.  And that is exactly why we have the protocols and procedures we have in place so we find these cases and contain them at the border. So just a summary then of the overall numbers, two previously reported cases have now considered to be recovered, and this brings our total number of active cases now to fifty-six.  The total number of confirmed cases is 1,556.  Yesterday the labs processed 6,308 tests and the grand total now is date to 1,040,911. I think that all of these cases, including the case we identified and announced over the weekend who became symptomatic on Friday as well as one of our contacts who became symptomatic yesterday reinforces our critical message, if you have symptoms do not go about your usual business, get a test, and that is a really key part of how we will continue to have an effective COVID-19 response.  Getting a test quickly allows us to rapidly identify any contacts, isolate and test them, and that is instrumental in us being able to contain any potential onward community spread.  It is also important that people continue to keep a record of where they have been.  Please use the NZ COVID app.  I know that for those of you who do, it is always a surprise when you go back and look and think gosh, I wouldn’t have remembered that I did that thirteen days ago. And my final comment is just again putting our twenty-five cases today in perspective, yesterday Spain announced around 48,000 new cases and that would be equivalent of New Zealand announcing nearly 4,000 cases in one day.  Spain is among a number of European countries that are experiencing well over a thousand new infections every day and they are implementing quite significant restrictions on movement and or going back into quite significant lockdowns, Ireland being a good example of the latter. So we have a significant pandemic, it is not going away anywhere soon.  We have done well so far, but all that matters now is what we do now and what we do next, and we all need to play our part in that.  We cannot afford to be complacent.  We are not being complacent at the border, and in managed isolation, and all New Zealanders need to make sure we are not being complacent in our community here in New Zealand.  We want to remain in the current state we are which is a high level of confidence we have eliminated community transmission of COVID-19.  I am now open to questions. --- Section Separator --- Media: You said New Zealand went over and above the regulations regarding testing crew before departure, would you now like to see that added to regulations for essential workers coming out to New Zealand? Dr Bloomfield: Well I think what we have got here is a really good opportunity to look at the value that pre-departure testing has or not.  And one of the things that is quite pertinent here and we are going through all the results, but it seems that all these workers did have pre-departure testing.  Now often that has to happen a few days before departure, especially if it is a PCR test, to get a result before they embark, and now we have clearly a number of positive results at day three that were negative at pre-departure testing, so what it suggests is that pre-departure testing may add some value but it won’t change anything that we do at this end and so I think the regime that we have with our fourteen days in managed isolation plus our days three and day twelve testing continues to be the mainstay of how we will keep the virus out of New Zealand. Media: Are there tight restrictions for the next lot of fishermen coming through? Dr Bloomfield: Are there tight restrictions?  We will look at that.  At this point in time, again the system is working here, and whilst there may still be the departure test it won’t change anything we do at this end, we will still be following absolutely rigid infection control or protocols and testing. Media: Those taking the test, can you explain how reliable those tests are? Dr Bloomfield: Well this is one of the challenges around pre-departure testing,  We don’t have any ability to test the validity of either the process, or the swabbing, or the laboratory or any rapid testing process so it does seem that the testing, and Caroline might want to comment on that, the nature of the testing that’s done. Dr McElnay: We are actually following that up with them.  From the information we have got it does appear to be a PCR test, a nasopharyngeal swab, and the real value of this pre-departure testing is when they test positive because obviously that’s what happened in this situation, and individuals then were not allowed to board the plane and come to New Zealand but for the negatives we just do not have access to that information to say really what is the accuracy of that test in that country Media: And are officials there being quite forthcoming with the information for you? Dr McElnay: I am not aware that there has been any issue. Media: Does the PCR testing … Dr Bloomfield: What it indicates is if they returned a negative test there if it was a good test in Moscow they got infected sometime after they were swabbed.  Now that could have been before they boarded the plane.  At least one must have been aboard that plane who was infectious and there may have been transmission on board the plane.  There may have been transmission once they arrived here.  But the important thing is we did everything the same anyway and the fact that we found these infections is evidence that the system worked. Media: ??? isolation Dr Bloomfield: My understanding is they self-isolated but again I think that, you know, our view would be of what self-isolation or quarantine arrangements might have been in place before people hopped on to the plane and whatever the testing results of the crew they are treated exactly the same when they come here because we have got real confidence in our systems here, both in the integrity of the managed isolation and the value of the day three and day twelve and any additional testing there needs to be, as well as the screening Media: Given that and the likelihood of it happening before coming here, the likelihood that someone was infectious on the plane, does that tell indicate that perhaps everybody was not self-isolating Dr Bloomfield: I couldn’t pass judgement on that but our assumption is and we know that even for the people with the best supportive environment it is quite hard to self-isolate really rigorously to the same degree of integrity that we know that we have in managed isolation.  And with that constant use of PPE, and the real discipline around infection prevention and control, the cleaning and so on, that is one of the key reasons why in early April we moved from self-isolation at home here in New Zealand to self-isolation in managed isolation. Media: Are you still comfortable that this kind of fishermen should come to New Zealand? Dr Bloomfield: Ah well at this point and we are liaising with both our fishery colleagues and obviously those in charge in managed isolation, we will learn again from this particular scenario.  The key thing will be the timing of that flight because we will need to make sure the same facility will be used so we need to make sure that we are confident that before anyone leaves that facility that with anyone that is there at the moment that they are free of the virus. Media: This cohort, any in medical care or needing hospitalisation?  Any communication with Christchurch Hospital over it? Dr McElnay: I haven’t had any of that information yet.  So far my understanding is that they are all able to be managed in the quarantine facility in Christchurch. Media: Are you still investigating active cases in that cohort or do you have all of them now? Dr Bloomfield: Amongst the cohort? Dr McElnay: They have all had their first round of testing which is how we have picked up the positive and then those who tested negative will have another test, a second round of testing tomorrow, day six, and they will proceed from there.  There may be some further positives tomorrow. Media: Yesterday Sealord said the Ministry of Health did not contact the infections, they learnt about them from the media.  Why is this? Dr Bloomfield: I would have to look into that, in fact one of the reasons I became aware of this is because my understanding was Sealord had already sent some communication out so regardless of how people found out the system worked, we got positive test results, and I have a detailed timeline of when folk in Christchurch notified our team which was about ten minutes after they had found out the results and you will have seen that out of cycle we actually made an announcement yesterday afternoon because of the importance of getting this information out there. Media: When did the fishermen come into isolation in the facility? Dr Bloomfield: It was Friday afternoon that their flight arrived and so effectively that is their day zero but because of these infections we will be looking at whether actually the clock may need to be re-set because we have had this quite large number of infections. Media: Do you generally trust the Russian authorities to give you straight answers about their COVID testing results? Dr Bloomfield: Well we haven’t had any interaction with the Russian authorities previously and nor have we on this.  The testing and the pre-departure arrangements were made by our professional companies here with their counterparts there in Russia so we haven’t had any actual interactions with the Russian authorities so I couldn’t comment on that. Media: Do you think in the future maybe you will look at other countries to bring their fishing crews from, is that a consideration? Dr Bloomfield: Not from my perspective because that is up to the companies and remembering that this arrangement had been very carefully planned and put in place with our colleagues from MPI. The approval had been provided by Ministers for this to happen, and there is another crew.  Remembering that they are placing crews on these New Zealand fishing boats and some of them have been on those boats for anything up to six – twelve months because of the COVID pandemic and that is why we have been very rigorous in the planning to make sure that we were reducing any risks to the crews and of course to New Zealanders. Media: Russia has had one of the largest outbreaks in the world and Russia has been accused of fudging the numbers right through the pandemic, so is it appropriate to be seeking crews from Russia? Dr Bloomfield: We have got people coming into New Zealand, mostly New Zealanders, from all around the globe including Europe, and from the USA which have large numbers. USA has the largest number of cases of any country in the world.  We treat everybody the same because we know that our procedures are in place to reduce the risk as low as possible of any infection getting into the country. Media: Do you know from the CT values which ones are most likely to be a historical infection versus new or in category where it is hard to tell? Dr McElnay: We don’t have that information but our public health colleagues in Christchurch will have that information and will be working through that. Dr Bloomfield: Maybe you could talk generally about the values and where is the most likely acute and most likely historical Dr McElnay: Certainly our observation is that from the CT values a high CT value can certainly suggest it is more likely to be an historic case and when we have had those, particularly ones that have appeared in the community where for some reason people have been tested, they have got a high CT value and intensive investigations around that person and ceretology we’ve been able to show and we’ve been reporting these for the last few weeks, we’ve been able to demonstrate that they are a historical case. In a situation like this, the need to investigate would really be to help determine who are contacts of cases of that historic case, you don’t have an issue to do with infectiousness of that individual so that is why this particular group would be a mixture of looking at who is historic versus those that are acute.  And for the acute ones that is particularly because they are actively infectious.  I want to reiterate what Ashley said earlier, our quarantine and isolation facilities are set up to manage infection prevention and control so we would assume that anyone could potentially be infectious at any time. Media: If it is a casual contact out of the cluster, is that ??? Dr Bloomfield: Again, on the basis of the history from the original case, which was found on Saturday night by the public health unit they judged this person as a casual contact because their interaction was fleeting. One of the things that we will now be looking at, in the public health unit they will be looking at, is because we now have the two work colleagues who are positive, it could have been the exposure of, the person may have been a casual contact of the original case but may have been a close contact of the other new cases and the person in Jet Park so again it is an opportunity to learn but remember what we do with casual contacts many of those we are testing up front but otherwise they have very clear instructions about what to do and especially if they become symptomatic as happened in this case. Media: Scan numbers for the tracer app have been going down for a while now, what is the Ministry doing to get people back to using that? Dr Bloomfield: Well two things, first I will be encouraging people every day and we will be pushing out the message for people to keep using it.  What we have seen is that when there is an imperative to use clearly kiwis are quite happy to use it.  But I think the other important point here is we are still developing the app and we are still actively looking at blue tooth functionality for it.  And I guess when we are in more of a sort of a, when we were in a high alert level and we have seen that in Auckland and around the country, people not only took up the app, but they were using it at high levels.  When we were in alert level one as we are now, that blue tooth functionality will be of additional value because it doesn’t require people to actively take their phone off and do the QR scanning so we are still looking at that – it will be one of the functionalities we are intending to add to the app, and I think again it’s value will particularly be of importance when at the higher alert levels. Media: Do you know what conditions were put on this crew and do you know if they were followed? Dr Bloomfield: What sort of conditions? Media: Like were MPI putting or Ministry of Health putting any conditions for this crew to fulfil to come over? Dr Bloomfield: Well I don’t have the details but as I said before there was some very rigorous planning around this including the requirement for the self-isolation, before they departed for the pre-departure testing, it was a dedicated charter flight, both legs, they were in a dedicated part of the facility, there is additional security being provided at the facility, so there were some bespoke arrangements for this crew and that was all arranged with the companies and with our colleagues in fisheries. Media: Beyond that do you feel there is industry that will want to bring cohorts of this size in the future, is there anything that you are learning from this experience that you think that you will have to tweak for next time as part of the planning? Dr Bloomfield: Well most certainly and most particularly for the next group of these fishermen that is due to arrive within a couple of weeks time as well, there will be plenty we will have learnt.  This isn’t the first group of essential workers that has come.  I think we have had vets, and we have had some agricultural workers that have come in but this is obviously the biggest group and again one of the challenges here is they have come with many of them are not English speakers, and so they have come from the Ukraine and Russia, and we have got interpreters involved a well, but all these things will help us learn. Media: Is there anything specific that you will already change for the future? Dr Bloomfield: I guess we are less than 24 hours into this one but what I can say, we have already, we are already changing for this cohort, is adding in the day six testing, so that is a new thing because of what has happened here. Media: You talked about the delay between testing and departure, do you know how much that is? Dr Bloomfield: I don’t have that information but if we find out we can let you know.  We will make that public. Media: And in terms of the day six testing, are they still being confined to their rooms? Dr Bloomfield: No they are not being confined to rooms.  And in fact they have been allowed out for breaks including cigarette breaks. Media: So is the day six testing because of that or are you concerned at the number of active people moving around the facility? Dr Bloomfield: Yeah, two reasons, one is of course that there is the twin-rooming which is unusual.  Usually people are either in family bubbles or in individual rooms, but secondly just to make sure that there hasn’t been any cross-contamination in those first few days because we have found that quite large number of positive results. Media: Do you know if there has been any cross-contamination? Dr Bloomfield: Well I don’t know exactly but what I can say is when we talked about this with colleagues this morning with the staff, there has been high levels of compliance, there has been the usual oversight security and support for infection and prevention control. Media: Those measures which you mentioned before which they are required to comply with, were they requirements that New Zealand put on them or were they requirements between the two countries? Dr Bloomfield: I think they were, what I would say is that they were arrangements that were agreed between New Zealand officials and the fishing companies to help facilitate getting this workforce over here in a way that was safe for New Zealanders.  And that is obviously a key priority. Media: Are their other international fishing crews in other facilities around New Zealand? Dr Bloomfield: No, not that I know of. Media: When you look at the bigger picture here though, there was supposed to be two weeks of self-isolation, then there was supposed to be testing before they left.  As you said those measures were put in place and within four or five days of arrival you have got more than 8% of the cohort testing positive, does that kind of strain the credibility that those things might not have actually happened? Dr Bloomfield: I think what it does is reinforce the importance of the protocols we have got here because you know just also by way of example, a number of the flights that come to New Zealand, particularly from the middle East, have pre-departure testing as well as the rapid testing at the airport but we still get positive so anybody that tests positive isn’t allowed to board the flight but we still get positive results here on day three testing so I think what it does show is that pre-departure testing may add value in some circumstances so that anyone that tests positive cannot board the flight but it doesn’t change what we do in New Zealand with our managed isolation.. Media: With the port worker and the contacts and so on, I don’t know if you will be able to answer this, but are there any ongoing investigations to like contacts of contacts? Any other workplaces needing to be scrutinized?  How many others may be out there? Dr McElnay: Auckland Regional Public Health have been actively following up further investigations from the two identified and reported today.  The focus to date has been around the port and the ships and the original case that we reported over the weekend.  The two ships that that case had been on.  So they are actively following those up.  With testing, we are taking a very proactive approach to contacts of contacts and we have certainly reiterated that message to Auckland Regional Public Health. Media: Family members of port workers, presumably they operate outside the port environment in the community.  Is there concern about that? Dr Bloomfield: There is not and I tell you why not.  Just to reiterate this is a very small company of four employees, it is not actually based at the port.  And so the only interaction that those workers have, even though they are described as port workers they are not based at the port – they work on the ships and the only interaction they really have at the port is with the security guards as they go through and straight to the ships.  Hence the focus on workplace contacts in the small workplace with four people and the ships and others who might have worked on the ships, particularly the one that is now in Brisbane, so the pilot and any stevedores that were involved in the work on that ship when it was in New Zealand, they have also been stood down and tested. Media: Many nurses that Newshub has spoken to have left their jobs in managed isolation sites after the DHB took over, they say their current shortages are dangerous for staff and internal documents confirm there is a shortage.  Do you have any concerns about this? Dr Bloomfield: I don’t, and I don’t share the same view, and in fact, if I give an example, across the managed isolation facilities in Auckland there are 175 nurses and health care assistants that work across there.  At the moment, as of last week actually, there were 120 of those employed by the DHBs and presumably they had taken up that role because they were comfortable with the work there.  There was still an arrangement in place to use 55 agency staff, that is Geneva, the company that has been involved right from the start in Auckland, so the DHB is moving to recruit to a fully DHB-employed workforce.  They are great people who are doing excellent work to help keep New Zealand safe. Media: We have been told of that the current claim - that nurse numbers is not safe and sufficient is not true and that nurses are burnt out.  What evidence do you have to suggest all is going well? Dr Bloomfield: I have great confidence in my colleague Margie Apa who is the Chief Executive of Counties Manukau District Heath Board and her team who are overseeing the clinical supervision and the clinical support of the nursing and the health care staff who work at quarantine facilities and managed care isolation.  What I do know is that if there are any issues they would be open to hearing those and to acting promptly on any of those issues. Media: Do you have any update on the crew of the ?? in Hawke’s Bay? Dr Bloomfield: That ship is still off-shore.  At the moment we are still considering them as close contacts and Caroline has got the latest information. Dr McElnay: Yes, because the port worker was on that ship during his infectious period as the people on that ship, we don’t consider them to be the source of the infection but we do consider that they have been close contacts so they are receiving daily checks, so that is underway, that has been happening since the weekend.  The ship is still off the coast at Napier, and we are just waiting on updates from the shipping company around where the ship will move to next. Media: Is there any concern? Dr McElnay: No, they are all symptom free. Dr Bloomfield: I think just the other comment to make on that is even if they are not tested in that fourteen day period, if they subsequently want to come on shore, anyone who comes on shore will require the whole crew to be tested and to get a test and to test negative. Media: How confident are you that you have got it contained now or not? Dr Bloomfield: Well it is contained in the sense that they are all in a managed isolation facility.  I would be surprised if we don’t find more cases either at the testing tomorrow or on day twelve, and whether those are acute or historical will be the important thing.  That is why we are testing at day six as an interim measure and then of course once we have got any results from that if that requires further testing or extension of that managed isolation period that will be one of the decisions that will be considered. Media: Is there any chance that it could be through the whole crew. That it has reached that point? Dr Bloomfield: No I don’t think so, because remember that once they have arrived in the facility even though they might be sharing rooms, there are very strict protocols in there and they are very carefully supervised and we have reports that there is a high level of compliance there.  And that is exactly why we have those arrangements in place. Media: We have been told that there are no dedicated swabbing teams any more.  On duty nurses are expected to do health checks and swabbing.  Is this a problem? Dr Bloomfield: No I don’t think it’s a problem. Media: Last week or maybe the week before there were a group of academics that released a statement.  Do you have any comment on that? Dr Bloomfield: Yes, I do know a bit about the statement and at the same time I have seen a bit of very credible and prominent public health leaders from around the globe take a compelling I think and opposing view.  And again I think the extent to which New Zealand has been successful in using lockdowns for a defined time and for the shortest time possible which a number of countries have done successfully, including Australia, and there are many others, I think is testament to the fact that lockdowns can and should play a place where necessary as part of a response.  But just to reiterate, the WHO view on this, which is not the first tool you should reach for in the bag, but in New Zealand as we have learnt more and more our strength and our border approach around testing, contact tracing, isolation, part of the reason for that is the less use of lockdowns in the future and I think with our management of the last two or three community-based infections, that is possible, and other countries have shown the same.  Did you want to comment Caroline? Dr McElnay: No Dr Bloomfield: She agrees with me. Media: The other ship that has been stopped from docking in Australia until we know more, you have said that is the likely source of infection.  Do we know any more about when we get more information about that.  Do you have ongoing discussions with Australia? Dr Bloomfield: Very intense discussions with Australia.  It is actually in Australia in Brisbane.  It got there two evenings ago.  The crew, all nineteen, have been tested.  We are just awaiting the results.  They are a couple of hours behind us timewise but I hope, I think people will recognise Queensland is one of the states that has got very strict internal border arrangements and restrictions and so they are obviously very keen to ensure that no-one in that crew is likely to be infectious and if they have positive results they will be genome sequencing to see if we can get a match with the cases that we have got here. Media: So will it be today or tomorrow that we will know the results of those? Dr Bloomfield: I would expect today and once we find out we will make that information available. Media: You have outlined previously why that seems to be contained and you see no immediate change to level or restrictions, what would need to happen for you to recommend that move? Dr Bloomfield: Well I think a move to level two would have been something that we might have advised when we first found the case if we were not clear where it had come from or if we had found some other cases coming out that we weren’t aware of but in this case we have a really strong line of inquiry for where this person might have become infected and we were able to identify very quickly their close contacts and isolate those people.  So, I think that is the system working as it should in the community. Media: So if the tests all come back negative, and you don’t see any link to that, does that scare you? Dr Bloomfield: Yes that is something that we discussed earlier on.  I mean, we will cross that bridge but it seems that in terms of timing and nature of the sub-type of the virus which is not one we’ve had in New Zealand before, it is not a common one from Australia or the UK, or the USA, it seems that that is almost certainly the most likely source of the infection. Dr McElnay: Just to add to that, every case has its own case investigation and it may be that the case that we reported today that had a common exposure to the one reported over the weekend and had gone to the same ship, it may be that there is something else in that individual’s history that gives us a stronger line of inquiry, those are the sorts of questions that Regional Public Health will be going over today just in case it looks like the Sofrana is not the source of infection. Media: Does it shine any more light for you on the Americold case that this is the possible source of infection for the three ships Dr Bloomfield: No it doesn’t shine any more light on that.  What it does confirm is that the virus can come in through anywhere in the border at any point in time and that is why we have continually strengthened our arrangements including the very now well-established surveillance testing of workers at sea ports and at airports.

The COVID-19 media conference was held at 1pm. Speakers: Director-General of Health Dr Ashley Bloomfield What the media conference here. --- Section Separator --- Update on today's cases from the Ministry of Health (external link)
Last month the New Zealand Government agreed to buy enough of the Pfizer BioNTech product to vaccinate 750,000 people. If Medsafe approves the vaccine for use in New Zealand, it is possible that some doses will be available here in the first part of 2021. Pfizer and BioNTech will keep us up to date on when data will be available for Medsafe to begin its evaluation process. Medsafe is working to ensure any promising vaccines will be fully assessed as quickly as possible against the same criteria used for all other medicines that enter New Zealand. This is just the first of several expected vaccine purchases which will create a portfolio of vaccine options for New Zealand. The COVID-19 Vaccine Strategy Taskforce, led by the Ministry of Business, Innovation and Employment, is working with COVAX to access vaccines and negotiating with other pharmaceutical companies. Further announcements are expected this month. The Ministry of Health is developing the process for rolling out vaccines as they become available and the approach will be finalised once the features of vaccines and the supply is understood. Three broad groups are being considered: those at risk of spreading COVID-19 those at risk of contracting the virus those with increased risk of serious illness or death from COVID-19. The government has set aside $66.3 million for medical supplies and infrastructure to ensure New Zealand is ready to launch a COVID-19 Immunisation Programme as soon as a safe and effective vaccine is available. COVID-19 vaccines in New Zealand
The Government has made an in-principle agreement with Janssen Pharmaceutica to buy up to 5 million COVID-19 vaccine doses. The agreement with Janssen is for up to 2 million vaccine doses from July 2021. There is also an option to buy up to 3 million more doses that will be delivered throughout 2022. The Janssen vaccine is likely to be a single dose, which may be more efficient to administer than other vaccine options. Janssen and its parent company Johnson & Johnson have a very strong history of producing safe and effective pharmaceutical products, giving us confidence they will develop, manufacture and deliver a safe and effective vaccine. We are working to make sure that extra agreements are in place to complete the vaccine portfolio. Our main priority is to make sure every New Zealander has access to safe and effective vaccines. Any COVID-19 vaccine used in Aotearoa must be approved by Medsafe. Safety will always be the priority — Medsafe will make sure any vaccine meets internationally agreed criteria for safety, efficacy and quality. This criteria includes data from well-designed clinical trials involving tens of thousands of people. Find out more about our vaccine strategy
If you are sick, do not travel or socialise this Labour weekend. If you are sick with cold, flu or COVID-19 symptoms, you should immediately self-isolate. This means you should not travel, and you should not go out to Labour weekend sales or other social or retail events. Instead, stay home and call your GP or Healthline on 0800 358 5453 to get a COVID-19 test. --- Section Separator --- Wherever you go and whatever you do over Labour weekend, record it. The NZ COVID Tracer app keeps us one step ahead of the virus. The more we all scan, the safer we’ll all be. If you see a QR code, scan it. This is particularly important as many people may be moving around the country, and you may not remember later the names and places of everywhere you visit if you are away from home. Even if you have fallen out of the habit of using the app, we urge you to start again — reset your habits and go from now. Businesses have a part to play as well. Make sure your QR code can be easily seen and accessed, and encourage people to scan in. If you do not use the app, remember to keep a pen and paper diary of where you’ve been and who you’ve seen. --- Section Separator --- When travelling, you are strongly encouraged to wear a face covering on public transport or planes, or when you cannot maintain physical distance in crowded indoor places. --- Section Separator --- Make sure you’re washing your hands regularly, keeping surfaces clean, coughing and sneezing into your elbow and wearing face coverings when you can’t maintain physical distance in crowded indoor spaces.
The case, reported to the Ministry on Monday afternoon, is a staff member working in a managed isolation facility in Christchurch where the international mariners are in managed isolation and quarantine. Full details of the case, and the actions taken in response will be provided at a 1pm media conference on Tuesday 3 November. The individual was tested as part of the routine testing for staff in the facility and returned a negative test on Thursday 29 October.  On Saturday, they developed symptoms and sought a further test on Sunday. A positive result was received today. The person is now in isolation at home and reports taking care to isolate themselves as soon as they developed symptoms. Advice if you’re in Canterbury region The first of the international mariners, due to complete their managed isolation on Tuesday morning, will have their managed isolation extended for at least 24 hours as an additional precautionary measure. Those precautionary measures have included additional tests — up to 4 tests for some individuals — and an already lengthened stay in managed isolation.
From 11:59pm on Wednesday 18 November, face coverings legally must be worn: on all public transport to, from and through Auckland on domestic flights throughout New Zealand by Auckland taxi and ride-share drivers — while it’s not compulsory for passengers to wear them, we strongly encourage you to. Children under the age of 12 will be exempt, as will people with certain medical conditions. Students will not need to wear masks on school buses, but drivers will be required to. A face covering helps stop droplets spreading when someone speaks, laughs, coughs or sneezes. This includes someone who has COVID-19 but feels well or has no obvious symptoms. Wear a face covering

If you were at the pub that night and get sick, even after a negative test result, you should self-isolate again and get tested again. Latest information on managed isolation charges (external link) --- Section Separator --- The COVID-19 Public Health Response Amendment Bill will provide a legal framework to allow the Government to only charge New Zealanders who enter temporarily, or who leave New Zealand after regulations come into force. Temporary visa holders would have to pay unless they were ordinarily resident in New Zealand before the border closure, and left before the border closure. --- Section Separator --- The Bill will provide a legal framework to allow the Government to set payment terms, exempt groups of people and waive charges in cases of financial hardship. It will also ensure that: recovered charges do not exceed the actual costs of managed isolation and quarantine no upfront payment would be required there would be flexibility in how people could pay. Check the progress of the COVID-19 Public Health Response Amendment Bill through Parliament (external link) --- Section Separator --- The proposed charging structure for managed isolation and quarantine will be: $3,100 per person in a room $950 for each additional adult, and $475 for each additional child sharing the room. The system has been designed so that it is fair on arrivals and not a barrier for returning to New Zealand, especially for those who might already be experiencing financial stress. It is forecast that more people will be travelling and arriving at the border. The Government has set aside a total of $479 million to pay for the costs of managed isolation facilities until the end of the year. Further details of the charging scheme, and when it will come into force, will be announced soon. --- Section Separator --- Once the regulations come into force, it is proposed New Zealanders, residents and temporary visa holders will have to pay charges: Any New Zealander, who either: leaves New Zealand after the regulations come into force, or is visiting New Zealand for less than 90 days. leaves New Zealand after the regulations come into force, or is visiting New Zealand for less than 90 days. Any temporary visa holder, unless: they were ordinarily resident in New Zealand as of 19 March 2020, and they departed New Zealand on or before 19 March 2020, and they are not entering New Zealand on a border exception as a critical worker. they were ordinarily resident in New Zealand as of 19 March 2020, and they departed New Zealand on or before 19 March 2020, and they are not entering New Zealand on a border exception as a critical worker. Flowchart: Proposed liability for managed isolation and quarantine charges [PDF, 216 KB] --- Section Separator --- Classes of people, such as the following, are proposed not to be liable for charges: Family members travelling or isolating with people who do not have to pay charges (unless they are entering New Zealand on a border exception as a critical worker). New Zealand citizens deported to New Zealand. Diplomats and official government representatives. Any person travelling to New Zealand to attend the sentencing of the accused in the Christchurch mosque attacks. Refugees and protected persons for their first entry into New Zealand after the charges come into force. Flowchart: Proposed liability for managed isolation and quarantine charges [PDF, 216 KB] --- Section Separator --- New Zealanders who are currently overseas (that is, who left before the regulations come into force) will not have to pay if they stay in New Zealand for 90 days or longer. If you leave New Zealand after the regulations come into force, it is proposed that you would have to pay for your managed isolation unless you qualify for a waiver or are otherwise exempt. Anyone who needs to come home but cannot afford the charges, will still be able to come home. It is proposed that regulations allow the chief executive of the Ministry of Business, Innovation & Employment to waive charges in full or in part if they consider that undue financial hardship will be caused by the payment of the charges, or in any circumstances (which could include compassionate grounds) for which they consider a full or partial waiver appropriate. This will allow the chief executive to take into consideration an individual’s full circumstances. Final decisions on this will be announced soon. --- Section Separator --- It is impossible to tell how the situation develops internationally and in New Zealand. Our priority is keeping New Zealand safe. The Government is working to make the system sustainable and manageable for as long as we need it and it is expected the system will be amended as appropriate. --- Section Separator --- Our experience from February and March 2020, before managed isolation and quarantine was introduced, showed that compliance with self-isolation requirements by people arriving in New Zealand was patchy and could not be relied on. Final decisions on this will be announced soon.
The Ministry of Health is confirming 1 new case of COVID-19 in a worker in the Auckland quarantine facility. Because the person works at the facility and is not a returnee they are considered a community case. The case was reported to the Ministry on Friday afternoon. The individual has been tested regularly as part of the routine testing for staff in the facility, most recently being swabbed on 3 November and returning a negative test on 4 November. The staff member developed symptoms on 5 November and was tested again that day. That test has returned a positive result. Contacts of the worker are being traced, isolated and tested. Two businesses in Auckland have been contacted by Auckland Regional Public Health Service (ARPHS) and notified that the worker visited their premises during their infectious period. A push notification has been sent via NZ COVID Tracer to everyone who scanned in to these businesses around the relevant time. Advice if you're in Auckland Full details of the case, and the actions taken in response to the positive result, will be provided in the 1pm update on Saturday. Managed isolation workers are regularly tested.  As in other recent cases this worker quickly got tested as soon as they became aware of symptoms. The early identification of cases along with rapid contact tracing assists in stopping any spread of the virus. Trick-or-treating on Halloween is a fun activity for many families in New Zealand. Here are some things you can do to keep you, your family and your community safe this Halloween. --- Section Separator --- If you feel unwell, stay at home. Call Healthline on 0800 358 5453 , your doctor or Nurse Practitioner, or your iwi health provider for advice about getting a test. COVID-19 tests are always free and are available across New Zealand. If you feel unwell, we encourage you not to go to Halloween events or activities, or host any in your home. --- Section Separator --- Try and keep your distance from people you do not know when you’re out and about on Halloween. It’s also a good idea to use hand sanitiser while you’re out. --- Section Separator --- If you’re out trick-or-treating, keep track of the houses you visit and the time you were there. The fastest way to do this is to use the NZ COVID Tracer app, but use any way that works for you. --- Section Separator --- If you expect visits from trick-or-treaters, create a QR code for your house to help them keep track of where they’ve been. Encourage trick-or-treaters to scan in. The NZ COVID Tracer app keeps us 1 step ahead of the virus — the more we scan, the safer we’ll be. Create your QR code poster by completing the online form. If you do not have a New Zealand driver licence, complete the Ministry of Health template instead. You do not need a New Zealand Business Number or business name if you're creating a QR code for personal use. Get your QR code poster using the online form (external link) Ministry of Health template if you do not have an NZ driver licence (external link)
The proposed Bluetooth contact tracing card, sometimes called the CovidCard, is now being trialled in Ngongotahā, just outside Rotorua. Between 500 and 1,500 members of the Ngongotahā community will participate in the trial. Registration began on 30 October, and the trial will finish on 15 November. Participants will be asked to wear the cards as they go about their daily activities and attend community events. The cards use Bluetooth to exchange “digital handshakes” and will keep an anonymised record of participants’ close contact with each other. The trial will help the Ministry of Health understand how well the contact tracing cards perform in a real-world scenario, whether they work with our contact tracing systems, and if people will accept and use them. The results of the trial will help the Government decide if we should use contact tracing cards alongside the NZ COVID Tracer app to support contact tracing. This phase of the trial was co-designed by the Ministry of Health, Te Arawa COVID-19 Response Hub and the Universities of Waikato and Otago. Te Arawa knows the Ngongotahā community better than anyone and has the skills and experience needed to support a successful community trial. Find out more about the trial, and register to participate if you live in Ngongotahā (external link)

For the most up-to-date information about our managed isolation and quarantine facilities visit the managed isolation and quarantine website. Get information about: getting a voucher to secure your place charges and exemptions facility locations what managed isolation is like Managed isolation and quarantine website (external link) --- Section Separator --- New Zealand is at Alert Level 1. The disease is contained in New Zealand, but uncontrolled overseas. It is safe for all business to operate as normal. It is safe for events and activities to go ahead. Controls exist at the borders to address the risk of importing COVID-19, and keep New Zealand safe. All people arriving in New Zealand are required to stay in a managed isolation or quarantine facility for at least 14 days and test negative for COVID-19. Other than the border measures there are no other restrictions in place for New Zealanders. There is a robust system in place to safely welcome New Zealanders home, while keeping our communities protected. You have a legal right to come home if you are: a New Zealand citizen a New Zealand resident with valid travel conditions. People from any other countries are unable to enter New Zealand at this time, unless they have specific grounds for exemption, such as being essential workers or for medical reasons. These people will need to apply to Immigration New Zealand for an exception to the border closure. Immigration NZ has the most up to date information on the border exception criteria and process (external link) Those returning to New Zealand must do their part to stop COVID-19 spreading in New Zealand. All arrivals must complete 14 days of managed isolation or quarantine and test negative for COVID-19 before they can enter the community. People without symptoms will isolate at a managed isolation facility, and those with symptoms or who test positive will isolate in a quarantine facility or separate area. Managed Isolation and Quarantine (external link) Carrying out managed isolation and quarantine throughout regions across New Zealand is a key part of our border management. All facilities used for isolation or quarantine are closely monitored to make sure all the measures are in place to keep New Zealand safe. New Zealand currently has managed isolation facilities in: Auckland Christchurch Rotorua Wellington Hamilton None of the hotels being used as managed isolation facilities are currently open to domestic travellers or guests — they are exclusive to people who have recently arrived in New Zealand. These facilities are operating safely and are carefully managed with security. The hotels are stand-alone facilities — there are no shared public spaces with other members of the community. In total there are more than 500 government and contracted staff directly supporting managed isolation and quarantine. Each quarantine and managed isolation facility has an All of Government support team. Each team works alongside facility management to support the health, wellbeing, security, supplementary logistics and administration of the people staying in the facility. The teams vary in size depending on facility capacity, and include people from the Ministry of Health, New Zealand Defence Force, Police, Aviation Security, and the Ministry of Social Development. Managed isolation and quarantine facilities meet a strict set of criteria to ensure people staying and working in them are kept safe, and that there’s a safe transition of people back into the community after their isolation period. Hotels are chosen based on demand and suitability. Selection includes an assessment by the national Isolation, Quarantine and Repatriation team, and site visits from our on-the-ground teams in the respective locations. Hotels must meet a range of criteria including security, entry/exit points, suitable room and bathroom facilities, provision of food and drink delivered to rooms, safe laundry protocols, and the ability to ensure the wellbeing of people. People arriving in New Zealand need to complete their at least 14-days stay in a managed isolation or quarantine facility to ensure they can return safely to their family and friends. People will be tested for COVID-19 around days three and 12 of their stay. They must also have a negative test before they are released. Managed isolation facilities are closely monitored to make sure all measures needed to keep the local community safe are in place. None of the facilities have shared spaces with the public or wider community. There is a robust process in place if a potential case is identified in an isolation facility, including isolation of any close or casual contacts. You may see staff on the ground in the community who work at managed isolation facilities. These people are following all public health guidance. People who are in managed isolation cannot leave the hotel until they complete 14 days and return a negative COVID-19 test. For everyone else, it is safe to be out and about in the community as normal. It is safe for all business to operate as normal. It is safe for events and activities to go ahead. Other than the border measures, including managed isolation and quarantine, there are no other restrictions in place for New Zealanders. Latest information on managed isolation charges (external link) --- Section Separator --- The Bill allowing the Government to recover some costs for managed isolation and quarantine passed its third reading today, and the charges will come into force as soon as regulations are finalised. The COVID-19 Public Health Response Amendment Bill and its supporting regulations will introduce a new charging system for managed isolation to balance the rights of New Zealanders to return home and help reduce pressure on the system. The proposed charges are: $3,100 for the first person in the room $950 for each additional adult sharing the room $475 for each additional child (aged 3 to 17 years) sharing that room. There will be no charge for children under the age of 3. These charges are GST inclusive. Any New Zealander who is currently overseas and enters New Zealand after the regulations come into force for less than 90 days will be liable for charges, unless they are exempt or qualify for a waiver. Any New Zealander who leaves New Zealand after the regulations come into force (for example for a holiday or business) will be liable for charges when they return, unless they are exempt or qualify for a waiver. Everyone entering New Zealand on a border exception as a critical worker will have to pay. In many cases, their employers will pay their managed isolation and quarantine costs. Other temporary visa holders are liable for the charges, unless they were ordinarily resident in New Zealand at the time the border closed and departed New Zealand on or before 19 March 2020. Charging will start soon after the regulations come into force. This will happen after the Bill is given Royal Assent. The start date of the charging regime will be published on www.miq.govt.nz (external link) as soon as it is confirmed. The following exemptions are proposed: Partners, dependent children and legal guardians who are isolating or travelling with someone who is exempt from paying charges, unless they are entering under a critical worker border exception. Someone in New Zealand who goes into managed isolation to care for a person who is exempt from charges. Anyone travelling to New Zealand to attend the sentencing of the person convicted of the Christchurch mosque attacks. Refugees, including claimants, protected persons and applicants under the special immigration category for victims of domestic violence, when they enter New Zealand for the first time. Anyone entering New Zealand after a medical air transfer or rescue at sea. Patients travelling as part of the Ministry of Health High Cost Treatment Pool or the Ministry of Foreign Affairs and Trade New Zealand Medical Treatment Scheme. New Zealand citizens ordinarily resident in the Cook Islands, Niue or Tokelau who are travelling to New Zealand for medical treatment. New Zealand citizens ordinarily resident in the Cook Islands, Niue or Tokelau, travelling from a third country through New Zealand (staying less than 90 days) in order to return to the Cook Islands, Niue or Tokelau (staying at least 90 days). People being deported to New Zealand, defined as “returning offenders” (in the Returning Offenders (Management and Information) Act 2015) and any New Zealand citizen deported from Australia. Diplomats and consular staff, including their families, and official government representatives. Waiver applications will be considered on a case-by-case basis. It is proposed that waivers will be available in cases of undue financial hardship and other special circumstances. These special circumstances could include: a New Zealander entering managed isolation because they left New Zealand to accompany a vulnerable or disabled person back to New Zealand someone needing to travel to or from New Zealand to receive medical treatment other compassionate grounds, including when a person has travelled to visit a seriously ill or dying close relative or attend a funeral or tangihanga, whether in New Zealand or overseas. People will be able to apply for waivers once the regulations are in force. Information about the process will be available on www.miq.govt.nz at that time. Returnees who need to pay will get an invoice at the end of their stay in managed isolation and will generally have 90 days to pay. Information about how to pay the fee will be provided on the invoice.

Home Updates and resources Legislation and key documents Proactive release --- Section Separator --- This release includes the papers, minutes and key advice for the decisions the Government has made relating to COVID-19 up to 10 August. A small number of documents and some parts of the released documents would not be appropriate to release and, if requested, would be withheld under the Official Information Act 1982 (the Act). Where this is the case, the relevant sections of the Act that would apply have been identified. Where information has been withheld, no public interest has been identified that would outweigh the reasons for withholding it. Some information has been withheld in full, from this release in relation to the relevant section(s) of the Act: Section 9(2)(f)(iv) — confidential advice Section 9(2)(ba)(ii) — information provided to the Government under an obligation of confidence Section 6(a) — international relations Section 9(2)(j) — commercial negotiations. Home Updates and resources Misinformation and scams --- Section Separator --- Beware of misinformation on social media and other sources. Only share information from official sources. Misinformation works against us at a time when we need to work together to beat the virus. You can always find find accurate and timely information on our website and through our social channels. Social media and newsletters The World Health Organization also provides a comprehensive list of COVID-19 information, including common misconceptions and misinformation. COVID-19 advice for the public: Mythbusters (external link) Scams and frauds exploiting COVID-19 have been noted overseas and are expected to be seen in New Zealand. They may arrive in the form of an email, text, phone call or through social media. For more information on what to watch out for and where to report scams, visit the following websites: COVID-19 investment scams: Financial Markets Authority (external link) COVID-19 phishing and cyber scams: CERT (external link) General COVID-19 scam information: CFFC (external link) Netsafe website (external link) Scamwatch: Consumer protection website (external link) It is not illegal for businesses to increase their prices; however, the Fair Trading Act prohibits misleading and deceptive conduct and false representations. This means that if a business gives a reason for a price increase it legally must be true, otherwise the business risks breaching the law. If you suspect a business is providing false or misleading information as to why prices have increased, you can make a complaint to the Commerce Commission. Make a complaint on the Commerce Commission website (external link) Complete the Price Watch form if you have concerns about price increases on essential goods or services. Price Watch form (external link) --- Section Separator --- Beware of misinformation on social media and other sources. Only share information from official sources. Home Updates and resources Social media and newsletters Social media community guidelines --- Section Separator --- We’re here to answer your questions about the COVID-19 virus and the government response. We welcome your comments and interactions but we have two simple rules for a safe social community: Be respectful to others. In the interest of maintaining a safe space for all New Zealanders, harmful content will be hidden or removed. This includes misinformation, swearing, hate speech, trolling and spam. If harmful content is repeatedly posted, then you may be banned or blocked. --- Section Separator --- We’re here to answer your questions about the COVID-19 virus and the government response. We welcome your comments and interactions but we have two simple rules for a safe social community: Be respectful to others. In the interest of maintaining a safe space for all New Zealanders, harmful content will be hidden or removed. This includes misinformation, swearing, hate speech, trolling and spam. If harmful content is repeatedly posted, then you may be banned or blocked. --- Section Separator --- Social media community guidelines
Home Updates and resources Translations Te reo Māori Te huaketo o te KOWHEORI-19 me ngā tohumate | The COVID-19 virus and symptoms --- Section Separator --- He mate korona hou te KOWHEORI-19 ka pā pea ki ō pūkahukahu me ō arahau. He maha ngā momo mate korona e puta ai ngā māuiuitanga pēnei i te maremare noa iho nei. maremare e hou ana, e kino kē ake ana rānei kirikā kāore e iti iho ana i te tohurau e 38 ( 38°C ) huatare o te manawa mātihetihe me te ihu hūpē korenga o te ihu e rongo ana i te kakara. Ina pāngia koe e ēnei tohumate, ehara pea i te mea kua āta pāngia koe e te KOWHEORI-19. Tērā pea kua pāngia kētia koe e tētahi mate e hōrapa ake ana, pēnei i te maremare, i te rewharewha rānei. Ko te huatare o te manawa tētahi tohu pea o te pūkahu kakā, ā, me mātua tirotiro e te tākuta. Tērā pea kua pāngia ētahi tāngata e ngā tohumate kāore e pērā rawa ana te hōrapa, pēnei i ēnei noa iho nei, i te: kirikā tikotiko māhunga ānini uaua mamae whakapai ruaki me te ruaki rangirua me te hōhā. Kāore anō mātou kia mōhio ki te roa o te wā ka hipa i mua i te kitenga o ngā tohumate mai i te pānga o te tangata. E ai ki te Kāhui Hauora o te Ao, kei te 2 – 10 rā tōna roa. Ka rere te KOWHEORI-19 mā ngā kōpata i tētahi tangata ki tētahi atu. Ka pāngia e te tangata mā ō rātou karu, mā ō rātou ihu, mā ō rātou waha hoki. Ka maremare ana rānei, ka matihe ana rānei, ka kōrero ana rānei tētahi kua pāngia e te mate, ka rere atu pea i a ia ngā kōpata kei roto rā te huaketo. He rahi rawa ēnei kōpata kia roa tonu te rere i te takiwā, nō reira ka tau ki runga i ngā mata huri noa. --- Section Separator --- He mate korona hou te KOWHEORI-19 ka pā pea ki ō pūkahukahu me ō arahau. Ka rere te KOWHEORI-19 mā ngā kōpata i tētahi tangata ki tētahi atu. Ka pāngia e te tangata mā ō rātou karu, mā ō rātou ihu, mā ō rātou waha hoki. Ka maremare ana rānei, ka matihe ana rānei, ka kōrero ana rānei tētahi kua pāngia e te mate, ka rere atu pea i a ia ngā kōpata kei roto rā te huaketo. He rahi rawa ēnei kōpata kia roa tonu te rere i te takiwā, nō reira ka tau ki runga i ngā mata huri noa. --- Section Separator --- Te reo Māori Ngā uwhi mata me ngā ārai | Face coverings and masks Te whakamātautau | Testing Ki te pāngia koe e te mate korona | If you test positive to COVID-19 Te huaketo o te KOWHEORI-19 me ngā tohumate | The COVID-19 virus and symptoms Te āhua o te āwhina a te whakatewhatewha pānga | How contact tracing helps Te noho i te pae 1 | Living at Level 1 Te noho i te pae 2 | Living at Level 2 Te tautoko i te whai kai, i te whai moni, i te whai kāinga, i te haumaru me te ora o te noho | Support with food, money, living, and staying safe and well He mea nui te whai i te pārongo tika | Getting the right information matters Ngā pānui whakaahua me ngā rauemi | Posters and resources Ngā uwhi mata me ngā ārai | Face coverings and masks Te whakamātautau | Testing Ki te pāngia koe e te mate korona | If you test positive to COVID-19 Te huaketo o te KOWHEORI-19 me ngā tohumate | The COVID-19 virus and symptoms Te āhua o te āwhina a te whakatewhatewha pānga | How contact tracing helps Te noho i te pae 1 | Living at Level 1 Te noho i te pae 2 | Living at Level 2 Te tautoko i te whai kai, i te whai moni, i te whai kāinga, i te haumaru me te ora o te noho | Support with food, money, living, and staying safe and well He mea nui te whai i te pārongo tika | Getting the right information matters Ngā pānui whakaahua me ngā rauemi | Posters and resources عربي | Arabic Te Reo Māori Kuki ‘Āirani | Cook Islands Māori Español | Spanish فارسی | Farsi Vosa Vakaviti | Fijian हिन्दी / Hindi 日本語 | Japanese Te taetae ni Kiribati | Kiribati 한국어 | Korean Vagahau Niue | Niuean ਪੰਜਾਬੀ / Punjabi Fäeag Rotųam | Rotuman Gagana Samoa | Samoan 简体中文 | Simplified Chinese Af-Soomaali | Somali Tagalog ภาษาไทย | Thai Te Gagana Tokelau | Tokelauan Lea Faka Tonga | Tongan Te Gana Tuvalu | Tuvaluan اُردُو | Urdu Tiếng Việt | Vietnamese
Home Updates and resources Translations Te reo Māori Ngā uwhi mata me ngā ārai | Face coverings and masks --- Section Separator --- Ko tā te mau uwhi mata he āwhina ki te aukati i te rerenga o te KOWHEORI-19, ā, mā te pērā hoki koe me ētahi atu e haumaru ai. Ka āwhina te uwhi mata i te aukatinga o te rere o ngā kōpata ka kōrero ana, ka kata ana, ka maremare ana, ka matihe ana rānei tētahi. Ka whai wāhi atu hoki ki tēnei te tangata kua pāngia e te KOWHEORI-19, engari e pēnei ana ia kei te pai tōna ora, kāore rānei ngā tohumate e āta kitea ana. Ehara i te mea me mau te nuinga o ngā tāngata i ngā ārai ā-hauora, ka mutu e pai ana kia mau rātou i ētahi atu momo uwhi mata. Ka kōrerotia te tangata me mātua whai i te uwhi mata, i te ārai ā-hauora rānei, e tōna kaituku mahi. Mehemea kāore ō uwhi mata, māu rānei tētahi e hanga, e hoko rānei. Ehara i te mea me mātua whai uwhi mata. Mā te mau uwhi mata i ngā wā kāore e taea te tīrara tonu i te hunga kāore i te mōhiotia e koe, pēnei i ngā wā kei te hokomaha, e haumaru ake ai koutou ko tō whānau. Me takatū tātou ina tūpono puta mai anō a KOWHEORI-19 ki te hapori. Ka pai kia whakaemihia he uwhi mata mō te katoa kei tō kāinga e noho ana i te nuinga o te wā. E whakatenatenahia ana te mau o te uwhi mata i ngā wā kāore e taea te tū tīrara tonu i te hunga kāore i te mōhiotia e koe, pēnei i te wā kei te hokomaha. Me mau hoki ngā uwhi mata e te nuinga o ngā tāngata kei ngā waka tūmatanui me ngā waka rererangi. Ka whai wāhi atu ki ērā ko ngā tereina, ko ngā pahi me ngā waka kōpiko. Ka whai wāhi atu hoki ko ngā kaihautū o ngā waka iti e kawe pāhihi ana pēnei i te tēkehi me Uber . Ehara i te mea me mau: e te tangata e hauā ana, e pāngia ana rānei e te mate hinengaro e uaua ai ki a rātou te uwhi i ō rātou mata e ngā tamariki kāore anō kia 12 tau te pakeke i runga i ngā pahi o te kura e ngā pāhihi o ngā waka iti e kawe pāhihi ana, pēnei i te tēkehi me Uber i ngā haerenga mā runga waka kua āta tāpuia, i ngā haerenga rānei o te tira tāpoi i ngā waka kōpiko i tētahi moutere ki tētahi i ngā rerenga tūmataiti e ngā kaikirimana tūmataiti e mahi nei i ngā ratonga rererangi, pēnei i te kaiwhakahaumako whenua mai i te rangi i ngā wā o te whawhati tata, i ngā wā rānei me taunaki rawa te tangata i tōna tuakiri, i ngā wā rānei me kōrero ki tētahi e turi ana. Mēnā ka pā tētahi o ēnei take ki a koe, ehara i te mea me whakaatu koe i tētahi tuhinga kotahi hei taunaki i te take kāore nei koe e mau ana i tētahi uwhi mata. Kāore tō haerenga mā te waka tūmatanui e aukatihia. He take whaimana tonu ā ētahi, pēnei i te take hauora, i kore ai e mau uwhi mata. Ehara i te mea me waea atu rānei ki te Pirihimana, me whakamataara rānei koe i te kaihautū rānei, i te kaiwhakahaere rānei, mehemea kua kite koe i tētahi kāore nei i te mau i tētahi uwhi mata. Ka āwhina te nuinga e kaha ana ki te mau uwhi mata i te rerenga o te mate, ka mutu ka mau rātou i ngā uwhi mata i ngā wā me pērā rātou. Mehemea e āta takahia ana e te tangata ngā ture mā te korenga e mau i tētahi uwhi mata i ngā waka tūmatanui me te kore i whaimana o te take, ka whiua pea ia ki te utu ā-hara o te $300, ki te utu rānei nā te kōti i whakatau, atu ki te $1000. Me mātua whai kia: mā maroke karukaru kore. mā maroke karukaru kore. I mua i tō mau i tō uwhi mata, horoia, whakamaroketia hoki ō ringaringa. Whakamahia ko te: hopi me te wai rānei patuero ā-ringa rānei (kia kaua e iti iho i te 60% te nui o te waipiro). hopi me te wai rānei patuero ā-ringa rānei (kia kaua e iti iho i te 60% te nui o te waipiro). Tukuna kia tūpoki te uwhi mata i tō ihu me tō waha, ka whakakikīngia ai mā ngā here rānei, mā ngā koropewa ā-taringa rānei. Ko te uwhi mata me: tūpoki katoa i tō ihu, i tō waha me tō kauwae hāneanea te mautanga, engari me piri hoki ki ngā taha o tō kanohi tuku kia māmā noa iho tō hā tūpoki katoa i tō ihu, i tō waha me tō kauwae hāneanea te mautanga, engari me piri hoki ki ngā taha o tō kanohi tuku kia māmā noa iho tō hā Horoia anō ō ringaringa. Whakamahia ko te: hopi me te wai rānei patuero ā-ringa rānei (kia kaua e iti iho i te 60% te nui o te waipiro). hopi me te wai rānei patuero ā-ringa rānei (kia kaua e iti iho i te 60% te nui o te waipiro). Parea te pānga ki te wāhanga o mua o tō uwhi mata, ki tō kanohi tonu rānei Parea te nekehanga o tō uwhi mata, tae atu ki te kumetanga ki raro iho i tō kauwae Whakakapia ki uwhi mata kē mehemea kua haukū, kua taretare, kua paruparu rānei. Horoia, whakamaroketia hoki ō ringaringa. Whakamahia ko te: hopi me te wai rānei patuero ā-ringa rānei (kia kaua e iti iho i te 60% te nui o te waipiro). hopi me te wai rānei patuero ā-ringa rānei (kia kaua e iti iho i te 60% te nui o te waipiro). Tangohia tō uwhi mata. Tangohia mai i muri, ka kumea atu ai i tō kanohi. Whakamahia ngā koropewa rānei, wetekina rānei ngā here. Kaua e pā ki te wāhanga o mua o te uwhi mata, ki ō karu, ki tō ihu, ki tō waha hoki. Horoia rānei, rukea atu rānei tō uwhi mata. Horoia ngā uwhi mata kua hangā i te papanga mā te pūrere horoi kākahu me te hopi kia 60 tohurau te wera o te wai. Āta whakamaroketia i mua i te whakamahinga anō. Kaua e whakamahia tētahi uwhi mata e haukū ana. Rukea atu ngā uwhi mata e rangitahi nei te whakamahia. Raua atu ki tētahi ipu para e whai taupoki ana, ki tētahi pēke whai piringa rānei, ka whiua atu ai. Kaua e whakamahia anō, e whai rānei ki te patu i ngā huaketo o ngā uwhi mata e rangitahi nei te whakamahia. Horoia ngā uwhi mata kua hangā i te papanga mā te pūrere horoi kākahu me te hopi kia 60 tohurau te wera o te wai. Āta whakamaroketia i mua i te whakamahinga anō. Kaua e whakamahia tētahi uwhi mata e haukū ana. Rukea atu ngā uwhi mata e rangitahi nei te whakamahia. Raua atu ki tētahi ipu para e whai taupoki ana, ki tētahi pēke whai piringa rānei, ka whiua atu ai. Kaua e whakamahia anō, e whai rānei ki te patu i ngā huaketo o ngā uwhi mata e rangitahi nei te whakamahia. Horoia anō ō ringaringa mā te whakamahi i te: hopi me te wai rānei patuero ā-ringa rānei (kia kaua e iti iho i te 60% te nui o te waipiro). hopi me te wai rānei patuero ā-ringa rānei (kia kaua e iti iho i te 60% te nui o te waipiro). --- Section Separator --- Ko tā te mau uwhi mata he āwhina ki te aukati i te rerenga o te KOWHEORI-19, ā, mā te pērā hoki koe me ētahi atu e haumaru ai.
